DEDICATION

To Monsieur Charles Nodier, member of the French Academy, etc.

My dear Nodier, here is a book of deeds hidden from the action of the laws behind the closed doors of domestic life; but in these affairs the finger of God, often called chance, supplies the place of human justice, and the moral, though sometimes pointed by a scoffer, remains striking and instructive.

Such deeds teach much for the Family and for Maternity. We may yet understand, perhaps too late, the effects of the diminution of paternal authority. That authority, which formerly ceased only at the father’s death, was the sole human tribunal before which domestic crimes could be arraigned; kings themselves sometimes took part in executing its judgments. However good and tender a mother may be, she cannot fulfill the patriarchal function any more than a woman can take the place of a king upon the throne. Perhaps I have never shown more plainly how essential to European society is the indissoluble marriage bond, how fatal feminine weakness can be, and how dangerous selfish interests are when indulged without restraint. May a society that worships wealth shudder to see the impotence of the law in dealing with a system that deifies success and pardons every means of attaining it. May it return to the Catholic religion for purification of its masses and an education other than that of a lay university.

In the “Scenes from Military Life” many noble self-devotions will be set forth; allow me to point out here the depraving effect of war’s necessities on minds who then act at home as if on the battlefield.

You have cast a sagacious glance over our time; your philosophy shines in many bitter reflections. You have appreciated, better than most, the havoc wrought in our minds by the existence of four distinct political systems. I cannot more fitly dedicate this history than under your protection. Your name may defend my work against the criticisms sure to follow it,—for where is the patient who keeps silence when the surgeon lifts the dressing from his wound?

To the pleasure of dedicating this Scene to you I add the pride of making known your friendship for one who here subscribes himself

Your sincere admirer,
De Balzac
Paris, November, 1842.

THE TWO BROTHERS

CHAPTER I

In 1792 the people of Issoudun had a physician named Rouget, whom they held to be consummately malignant. Rumor said he made his wife miserable, though she was the neighborhood beauty. Whether she was silly or not, friends and enemies never laid bare that household. The doctor was a man of whom you say, “He is not pleasant to deal with,” and so townsmen treated him civilly. His wife, a demoiselle Descoings, weak in health as a girl (said to be why the doctor married her), bore a son and, ten years later, a daughter, Agathe, whose birth surprised even the doctor.

These simple facts must be told, for without them a man like Rouget would appear a monster, when in fact he followed the evil tendencies many shelter under the axiom that “men should have strength of character,” a phrase that has caused much female misery.

The elder Descoings, father-in-law and mother-in-law of the doctor, were commission merchants in the wool trade, buying for manufacturers and selling for producers, pocketing commission on both sides, and growing rich and miserly. Their son Descoings disliked Issoudun, went to Paris, set up as a grocer in the rue Saint-Honore, and there, by love and patience and a little money from his parents, married the widow of his predecessor, Monsieur Bixiou.

In 1792 Descoings seemed to prosper. The old Descoings had retired and invested in buying forfeited estates. Doctor Rouget, foreseeing his wife’s death, sent Agathe to Paris to his brother-in-law both to let her see the capital and to carry out a scheme of his own: he judged that his brother-in-law and sister, childless and older, might grow fond of the child. Agathe, the handsomest maiden of Issoudun, did not resemble either parent. Her birth had caused a breach between Doctor Rouget and his friend Monsieur Lousteau, a former sub-delegate who left town when sub-delegates were suppressed. Madame Rouget spent much time with Madame Hochon, Lousteau’s sister and Agathe’s godmother, to whom she confided her griefs; Madame Hochon later told most of Issoudun what it knew of Madame Rouget.

When Rouget told his wife he would send Agathe to Paris her first words were, “I shall never see my daughter again.” Madame Hochon thought she was right. The poor mother grew yellow with grief; tongues said the doctor was killing her by inches. The behavior of her foolish son Jean-Jacques, who owed her little attention or respect, added to her misery. Jean-Jacques resembled his father in the worst ways; the doctor himself was unsatisfactory at best.

Agathe’s arrival did not bring happiness to her uncle. In the same period Descoings was imprisoned on a hint from Robespierre. He had expressed doubts of the famine to customers in his shop, and citoyenne Duplay, Robespierre’s housekeeper, took offense and denounced him for “accaparation.” His wife’s ill-judged efforts to obtain his release only made matters worse. She knew Bridau, secretary to Roland, and put him on the war-path. That incorruptible official sought to enlighten the arbiters, but they only replied, “Why do you meddle?” Bridau advised patience. Madame Descoings fretted and even appealed to a member of the Convention, who promised to speak to Robespierre and then forgot. A little sugar or liqueur to citoyenne Duplay would likely have saved Descoings. The lesson of those days is that it is as dangerous to employ honest men as scoundrels; one must rely on oneself. Descoings perished on the scaffold with André Chénier. His death produced more sensation then than Chénier’s; it has taken thirty years to prove France lost more by Chénier’s death than by Descoings’s.

That neighborhood shop, so near Robespierre’s lodging, seemed cursed: Cesar Birotteau later failed there as well. During the visits Bridau paid the widow, he was struck by Agathe’s cold, calm, innocent beauty and married her with the consent of her father. Doctor Rouget, eager to secure his design, hurried to Paris to draw the marriage contract to his advantage. Bridau’s ardent and disinterested love gave carte blanche to the perfidious doctor, who made full use of his son-in-law’s blindness, as this history will show.

Madame Rouget, or rather the doctor, inherited the elder Descoings’ property; within two years both had died, and Rouget, after his wife’s death in 1799, acquired vineyards, farms, and iron-works. Jean-Jacques proved incapable of doing anything; the father destined him for the life of a proprietor and allowed him to grow up in wealth and silliness. After 1799 the cipherers of Issoudun put at least thirty thousand francs’ income to the doctor’s credit. From his wife’s death he led a debauched life, kept within his house; this man of “strength of character” died in 1805. Jean-Jacques remained a bachelor for certain reasons partly his father’s fault, as will appear.

It is well to ask the result of the secret vengeance the doctor took on a daughter he did not recognize as his own, though legitimately his. Agathe bore a strong likeness to the doctor’s mother; resemblances sometimes skip generations, as vexing to science as any other mystery of heredity. In turn, Agathe’s eldest child, who resembled her physically, possessed the moral qualities of his grandfather, Doctor Rouget.

Agathe’s beauty was of a pure, placid kind—an oval face, clear whiteness of complexion, golden hair, composed mouth, long lashes, and tender dark-blue eyes. Bridau’s portrait of her hangs in his atelier. She was the ideal provincial housekeeper, pious though not sanctimonious, with an education given by the Church. In ordinary terms she was an accomplished wife, yet her ignorance of life opened the way to misfortunes; the Roman epitaph “She did needlework and kept the house” fitted her.

Under the Consulate Bridau adored Napoleon and in 1804 became head of a department at the ministry of the interior with a salary of twelve thousand francs and handsome emoluments. From 1804 to 1808 the Bridaus lived in a handsome suite on the Quai Voltaire, near the ministry and the Tuileries. A cook and footman were their only servants. Agathe, early afoot, went to market with her cook and prepared breakfast; Bridau never went to the ministry before eleven. When together, she prepared his breakfast, watched him off at the window, cleared the table, dressed, played with the children, and later sat silent, knitting by his writing table when he worked late. Sometimes they attended the theatre or used a ministerial box; Agathe enjoyed the occasional restaurant as any provincial new to Paris. Bridau received friends Thursdays and gave an annual Shrove-Tuesday ball.

Their conjugal life had but three events: the birth of two children three years apart and Bridau’s death in 1808, killed by overwork just as Napoleon was about to appoint him director-general, count, and councillor of state. Napoleon, who had been impressed by Bridau’s devotion, was about to reward him when the effort to finish a work before the Emperor’s departure caused an inflammatory fever that killed him. Touched by a devotion that received none of the brilliant recognitions given to soldiers, Napoleon planned an order to requite civil services, the Order of the Reunion, worn with a blue ribbon, though Providence thwarted that profanation. After Bridau’s death the Emperor inquired into his widow’s circumstances: both sons received scholarships at the Imperial Lyceum paid from the Emperor’s privy purse, and Madame Bridau was given a pension of four thousand francs.

From her marriage to her husband’s death Agathe had had no communication with Issoudun. Her mother died as she was about to bear her youngest son, and when her father died, the coronation of the Emperor kept Bridau busy and Agathe unwilling to leave him. Her brother Jean-Jacques had not written. Once a year she received a letter from her godmother Madame Hochon, to which she replied with commonplaces and paid little heed to the pious advice therein.

Some time before Doctor Rouget’s death Madame Hochon warned Agathe she would get nothing from her father’s estate unless she gave a power of attorney to Monsieur Hochon. Agathe was reluctant to harass her brother. Whether because Bridau thought the spoliation legal or because he shared his wife’s magnanimity, he would not listen to Roguin, his notary, who urged him to use his ministerial position to contest the deeds by which the father had deprived his daughter. Thus husband and wife tacitly sanctioned the transfer. Roguin, however, forced Bridau to reflect that if he died first Agathe would be left without property. Reviewing their finances, Bridau found that between 1793 and 1805 they had used nearly thirty thousand of the fifty thousand francs that old Rouget had given to Agathe at her marriage. He invested the remaining twenty thousand in the public funds then quoted at forty, from which Agathe received about two thousand francs a year. As a widow, she could live suitably on an income of six thousand francs. With provincial good sense she planned to change residence, dismiss the footman, and keep only a cook; but her intimate friend Madame Descoings, insisting on being considered her aunt, sold her own establishment and came to live with Agathe, taking Bridau’s study as her bedroom.

The two widows pooled their revenues, possessing jointly about twelve thousand francs a year. Such simple, “natural” measures often deserve attention, for fools excuse themselves by calling wrongs natural. Behold the serpent amid flowers.

In 1809 Madame Descoings, who never told her age, was sixty-five. Once called a beauty, she had kept her looks by constitution; medium height, plump, fresh, with fine shoulders and a rather rosy complexion, her blond hair showed no gray despite her husband’s fate. She loved good cheer, the theatre, and a secret vice—lottery shares. Like women lucky enough to stay young, she spent on dress and was the pleasantest of companions, never opposing anyone and giving and taking a jest. If she did not marry again, it was no doubt the times: during the Empire marrying men found young women too easily.

Madame Descoings often cheered Madame Bridau with theatre and drives, prepared excellent dinners, and even tried to marry her to her own son by her first husband, Bixiou. To do this she revealed a family secret carefully kept by her, her late husband, and their notary: her son Bixiou, thirty-five and a widowed major who later perished at Lützen, had a son—a grandson she secretly saw and claimed as the child of her first wife. That boy, educated with Agathe’s sons at the Imperial Lyceum on a half-scholarship, proved clever, witty, and gifted as a draughtsman and designer.

Agathe, who lived only for her children, declined to remarry out of fidelity to her husband and good sense. But being a good mother is harder than being a good wife; a widow must both love and command her children. Few women are firm enough to perform that double duty. Thus Agathe, despite many virtues, was the innocent cause of grave unhappiness. Through her lack of intelligence and blind confidence, she fell victim to Madame Descoings, who nursed a combination of three numbers in a lottery and used the joint household money to pay stakes, hoping at last to win and enrich her grandson, Agathe, and the little Bridaus.

When the debts reached ten thousand francs she increased her stakes; failing to win, the debt rolled up. At twenty thousand francs she lost her head, still hoping for a trey. She attempted to mortgage her property to help, but Roguin, the notary, showed that the late Doctor Rouget had secured her a life-interest only by disinheriting Madame Descoings in favor of his son Jean-Jacques. No money-lender would advance twenty thousand francs to a sixty-six-year-old on an annuity of about four thousand when ten percent could be obtained elsewhere. One morning Madame Descoings fell at Agathe’s feet and, sobbing, confessed the state of things. Agathe reproached her not; she dismissed the footman and cook, sold all but the bare necessities of her furniture, sold three-fourths of her government funds, paid the debts, and bade farewell to her appartement.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER II

One of the worst corners in all Paris is that part of the rue Mazarin between the rue Guenegard and its junction with the rue de Seine, behind the palace of the Institute. The high gray walls of the college and of the library Cardinal Mazarin gave the city cast chill shadows over this angle, where the sun seldom shines and the north wind blows. The poor ruined widow came to live on the third floor of a house standing at this damp, dark, cold corner. Opposite rose the Institute buildings, which contained the dens of ferocious animals known to the bourgeoisie as artists—tyros, or rapins, in the studios. Into these dens rapins enter, but they may come forth prix de Rome. The transformation does not take place without extraordinary uproar at the time of year when the examinations are going on and competitors are shut up in their cells. To win a prize, a sculptor had to make a clay model within a given time; a painter, a picture as at the Ecole des Beaux-Arts; a musician, a cantata; an architect, plans for a public building. At the time we write, this menagerie has already been removed from these cold and cheerless buildings to the elegant Palais des Beaux-Arts nearby.

From Madame Bridau’s new windows one could penetrate the depths of those melancholy barred cages. To the north, the view was shut in by the dome of the Institute; looking up the street, the only distraction was a file of hackney-coaches at the upper end of the rue Mazarin. After a while the widow put boxes of earth before her windows and cultivated those aerial gardens that police regulations forbid, though their vegetables purified the atmosphere. The house, which backed upon another fronting on the rue de Seine, was necessarily shallow, and the staircase wound round upon itself. The third floor was the last. Three windows opened to three rooms—dining-room, a small salon, and a chamber on one side of the landing; on the other, a little kitchen and two single rooms; above, an immense garret without partitions. Madame Bridau chose this lodging for three reasons: economy, for it cost only four hundred francs a year so she took a nine-year lease; proximity to her sons’ school, the Imperial Lyceum being near; and because it was in the quarter she was used to.

The interior matched the outside. The dining-room, hung with yellow paper patterned with small green flowers and floored with unglazed tiles, contained only a table, two sideboards, and six chairs brought from the other appartement. The salon was adorned with an Aubusson carpet given to Bridau when the ministry of the interior was refurnished. To this furniture the widow added a common mahogany sofa with Egyptian heads made by Jacob Desmalter, covered in a green silk with white geometric circles. Above it hung a pastel portrait of Bridau by an amateur, which at once attracted the eye. Though art might have objections, no one could fail to recognize the firmness of the noble, obscure citizen upon that brow. The serenity of the eyes, gentle yet proud, the sagacious mind testified by prudent lips, the frank smile, the atmosphere of the man of whom the Emperor had said “Justum et tenacem”—all had been caught, if not with talent, at least with fidelity. An observer could see that the man had done his duty. His countenance bore signs of the incorruptibility we attribute to those who served the Republic. On the opposite wall, over a card-table, flashed a picture of the Emperor in brilliant colors by Vernet; Napoleon was riding rapidly, attended by his escort.

Agathe had given herself two large birdcages—one with canaries, the other with Java sparrows. She had adopted this childish fancy since her husband’s loss, irreparable to her and to many others. By the end of three months her widowed chamber had become what it would remain until she left it forever—a litter beyond description. Cats domiciled on the sofa; the canaries, occasionally let loose, left their commas on the furniture. The poor woman scattered little heaps of millet and bits of chickweed about the room and put tidbits for the cats in broken saucers. Garments lay everywhere. The room breathed of the provinces and constancy. Everything of Bridau’s was scrupulously preserved. Even the implements in his desk received the care a paladin’s widow might have given her husband’s armor. One slight detail reveals her devotion: she had wrapped up a pen, sealed the package, and written, “Last pen used by my dear husband.” The cup from which he drank his last draught was on the fireplace; caps and false hair were later tossed over the glass globes covering these relics. After Bridau’s death, not a trace of coquetry, not even ordinary care of her person, remained in the young widow of thirty-five. Parted from the only man she had known, esteemed, and loved—one who had never caused her the slightest unhappiness—she was no longer conscious of her womanhood; all things were nothing to her; she no longer even thought of dress. Nothing was ever more simply done or more complete than this renunciation of conjugal happiness and personal charm. Some people, through love, transfer the self to another; when death takes that other, no life of their own is possible.

Agathe now lived only for her children and was infinitely sad at the privations this ruin would bring them. From her removal to the rue Mazarin a shade of melancholy came upon her face, making it very touching. She hoped a little in the Emperor; but the Emperor could do no more than he was already doing—he gave three hundred francs a year to each child from his privy purse, besides scholarships.

As for the brilliant Descoings, she occupied an appartement on the second floor similar to her niece’s above. She had assigned Madame Bridau three thousand francs out of her annuity. Roguin, the notary, attended to this in Madame Bridau’s interest, but it would take seven years of slow repayment to make good the loss. The Descoingses, thus reduced to an income of twelve hundred francs, lived with her niece in a small way. These timid creatures employed a woman-of-all-work for the morning only. Madame Descoings liked to cook and prepared the dinner. In the evenings a few old friends, persons employed at the ministry who owed their places to Bridau, came for a game of cards with the two widows. Madame Descoings still cherished her trey, which she declared was obstinate about turning up. She hoped, by one grand stroke, to repay the enforced loan she had made upon her niece. She was dearer to the little Bridaus than her grandson Bixiou—partly from a sense of wrong she had done them, partly because she felt the kindness of her niece, who, under her worst deprivations, never uttered a word of reproach. So Philippe and Joseph were cosseted, and the old gambler of the Imperial Lottery (like others who have a vice to atone for) cooked them nice little dinners with plenty of sweets. Later, Philippe and Joseph could extract small sums from her pocket with ease—the younger to buy pencils, paper, charcoal and prints, the elder for tennis-shoes, marbles, twine, and pocket-knives. Madame Descoings’s passion forced her to be content with fifty francs a month for domestic expenses so she could gamble with the rest.

Madame Bridau, by motherly love, kept her expenses down to the same sum. By way of penance for her former overconfidence, she heroically cut off her little enjoyments. As with other timid souls of limited intelligence, one shock roused her distrust and she exaggerated a defect until it assumed the consistency of a virtue. The Emperor, she said to herself, might forget them; he might die in battle; her pension ceased with her life. She shuddered at the risk her children ran of being left alone without means. Quite incapable of understanding Roguin when he explained that in seven years Madame Descoings’s assignment would replace the money she had sold out of the Funds, she persisted in trusting neither the notary nor her aunt, nor even the government; she believed only in herself and the privations she practiced. By laying aside three thousand francs every year from her pension she would have thirty thousand at the end of ten years, which would give fifteen hundred a year to her children. At thirty-six she might expect to live twenty years longer; and if she kept to the same system she might leave each child enough for bare necessities.

Thus the two widows passed from hollow opulence to voluntary poverty—one under the pressure of a vice, the other through the promptings of the purest virtue. None of these petty details are useless in teaching the lesson of this history, drawn from the commonplace interests of life but whose bearings are perhaps more widespread. The view from the windows into the student dens; the tumult of the rapins below; the necessity of looking up at the sky to escape the miserable sights of the damp angle of the street; the presence of that portrait, full of soul and grandeur despite the workmanship of an amateur; the sight of rich colors, now old and harmonious, in that calm home; the mother’s preference for her eldest child; her opposition to the younger’s tastes; in short, the body of facts and circumstances that preface this history are perhaps the generating causes to which we owe Joseph Bridau, one of the greatest painters of the modern French school.

Philippe, the elder son, strikingly resembled his mother. Though a blond lad with blue eyes, he had the daring look readily taken for intrepidity and courage. Old Claparon, who entered the ministry with Bridau and was one of the faithful whist players with the widows, used to say, tapping Philippe’s cheek, “Here’s a young rascal who’ll stand to his guns!” The boy, thus stimulated, naturally assumed a resolute manner. That turn once given to his character, he became very adroit at bodily exercises; his fights at the Lyceum taught him endurance and contempt for pain, which laid the foundation of military valor. He also acquired, naturally, a distaste for study; public education failed to develop body and mind pari passu.

Agathe believed that the physical resemblance between Philippe and herself carried a moral likeness; she confidently expected him to show her own delicacy of feeling heightened by manly vigor. Philippe was fifteen when the family moved to the rue Mazarin; the winning ways of a lad of that age confirmed maternal belief. Joseph, three years younger, was like his father, but only on the defective side. His thick black hair was always in disorder; Philippe’s, despite vivacity, was invariably neat. By some fatality Joseph could not keep his clothes clean; dress him in new clothes and he made them look old. The elder took care of his things out of vanity. Unconsciously, Agathe acquired the habit of scolding Joseph and holding up his brother as an example. She did not treat them alike; when she fetched them from school the thought about Joseph always was, “What sort of state shall I find him in?” These trifles drove her into maternal preference.

No one among the ordinary circle—neither old Du Bruel nor Claparon, nor Desroches the father, nor even the Abbé Loraux, Agathe’s confessor—noticed Joseph’s faculty for observation. Absorbed in his own tastes, the future colorist paid no attention to anything concerning himself. During childhood this disposition was so like torpor that his father feared he might suffer hydrocephalus. His face, whose originality was thought ugliness by those without an eye for the moral value of a countenance, wore a sullen expression in childhood. The features, which developed later, were pinched, and the close attention Joseph paid to what went on about him contracted them further. Philippe flattered his mother’s vanity, but Joseph won no compliments. Philippe sparkled with clever sayings and lively answers that lead parents to believe their boys will turn out remarkable men; Joseph was taciturn, a dreamer. The mother hoped great things of Philippe, and expected nothing of Joseph.

Joseph’s predilection for art began with a commonplace incident. During the Easter holidays of 1812, coming from a walk in the Tuileries with his brother and Madame Descoings, he saw a pupil drawing a caricature of a professor on the wall of the Institute and stopped in admiration at the charcoal sketch, full of satire. The next day he stood at the window watching pupils enter the building by the rue Mazarin door; then he slipped into the long courtyard of the Institute, full of statues, busts, half-finished marbles, plasters, and baked clays; at all of which he gazed feverishly—his instinct awakened, his vocation stirred. He entered a ground-floor room with a half-open door and saw a dozen young men drawing from a statue, who at once began to make fun of him.

“Hi! little one,” cried the first, taking crumbs of his bread and scattering them at the child.

“Whose child is he?”

“Goodness, how ugly!”

For a quarter of an hour Joseph stood and bore much teasing in Chaudet’s atelier. But after laughing at him the pupils were struck by his persistency and expression. They asked what he wanted. Joseph answered he wished to learn to draw; they encouraged him. Won by this friendliness, the child told them he was Madame Bridau’s son.

“Oh! if you are Madame Bridau’s son,” they cried, “you will certainly be a great man. Long live the son of Madame Bridau! Is your mother pretty? If you are a sample of her, she must be stylish!”

“Ha! you want to be an artist?” said the eldest pupil, coming up to Joseph, “but don’t you know that requires pluck; you’ll have to bear all sorts of trials—enough to break your legs and arms and soul and body. All the fellows you see here have gone through ordeals. That one, for instance, he went seven days without eating! Let me see if you can be an artist.”

He took one of the child’s arms and stretched it straight up; then placed the other as if Joseph were delivering a blow.

“Now that’s what we call the telegraph trial,” said the pupil. “If you can stand like that without lowering or changing position for a quarter of an hour, then you’ll have proved yourself a plucky one.”

“Courage, little one, courage!” cried the rest. “You must suffer if you want to be an artist.”

Joseph, with the good faith of thirteen, stood motionless for five minutes, all the pupils gazing solemnly. “There! you are moving,” cried one. “Steady, steady, confound you!” cried another. “The Emperor Napoleon stood a whole month as you see him there,” said a third, pointing to a fine Chaudet statue in the room representing the Emperor with the Imperial sceptre, which had been torn down in 1814 from the column it surmounted.

At the end of ten minutes sweat stood on Joseph’s forehead. At that moment a bald-headed little man, pale and sickly, entered; respectful silence reigned.

“What are you about, you urchins?” he exclaimed as he looked at the youthful martyr.

“That is a good little fellow, who is posing,” said the tall pupil.

“Are you not ashamed to torture a poor child in that way?” said Chaudet, lowering Joseph’s arms and giving him a friendly pat. “How long have you been standing there?”

“A quarter of an hour.”

“What brought you here?”

“I want to be an artist.”

“Where do you belong? where do you come from?”

“From mamma’s house.”

“Oh! mamma!” cried the pupils.

“Silence at the easels!” cried Chaudet. “Who is your mamma?”

“She is Madame Bridau. My papa, who is dead, was a friend of the Emperor; and if you will teach me to draw, the Emperor will pay all you ask for it.”

“His father was head of a department at the ministry of the Interior,” Chaudet said, struck by recollection. “So you want to be an artist, at your age?”

“Yes, monsieur.”

“Well, come here just as much as you like; we’ll amuse you. Give him a board, paper, and chalks, and let him alone. You are to know, you young scamps, that his father did me a service. Here, Corde-a-puits, go and get some cakes and sugar-plums,” he said to the pupil who had tormented Joseph, giving him small change. “We’ll see if you are to be artist by the way you gobble the dainties,” added the sculptor, chucking Joseph under the chin.

Then he went round examining the pupils’ works, followed by the child, who looked and listened and tried to understand. The sweets were brought—Chaudet, the child, and the studio all had their teeth in them; Joseph was petted as much as he had been teased. The rough play and real heart of artists revealed themselves, and the scene impressed his mind. The apparition of the sculptor—for whom the Emperor’s protection opened a way to future glory, closed soon after by premature death—was like a vision to little Joseph. He told his mother nothing of this adventure, but he spent two hours every Sunday and Thursday in Chaudet’s atelier. From then Madame Descoings, who humored the cherubim’s fancies, kept Joseph supplied with pencils, red chalks, prints and drawing-paper. At school the future colorist sketched his masters, drew comrades, charcoal-sketched dormitories, and showed surprising assiduity in the drawing-class. Lemire, the drawing-master, struck by the lad’s inclination and progress, told Madame Bridau of her son’s faculty. Agathe, like a true provincial who knows little of art, was terrified. When Lemire left her, she burst into tears.

“Ah!” she cried to Madame Descoings, “what is to become of me! Joseph, whom I meant to make a government clerk, whose career was marked out at the ministry of the interior, where, protected by his father’s memory, he might have risen to be chief of a division before twenty-five, he wants to be a painter—a vagabond! I always knew that child would give me nothing but trouble.”

Madame Descoings confessed she had encouraged Joseph’s passion for months, aiding his Sunday and Thursday visits to the Institute. At the Salon, to which she had taken him, the boy’s interest in pictures had been, she declared, miraculous.

“If he understands painting at thirteen,” she said, “your Joseph will be a man of genius.”

“Yes; and see what genius did for his father—killed him with overwork at forty!”

At the close of autumn, as Joseph entered his fourteenth year, Agathe, contrary to Madame Descoings’s entreaties, went to see Chaudet and requested he cease to debauch her son. She found the sculptor in a blue smock, modelling his last statue; he received the widow of the man who had once served him rather roughly; but already at death’s door, he was working with passionate ardor to accomplish in hours what he might have done in months. As Madame Bridau entered, he had just found an effect long sought and was handling tools and clay with spasmodic jerks that seemed to the ignorant Agathe like those of a maniac. At any other time Chaudet would have laughed; but now, hearing the mother bewail the destiny he had opened to her child, abusing art and insisting Joseph be barred from the atelier, he burst into holy wrath.

“I was under obligations to your deceased husband, I wished to help his son, to watch his first steps in the noblest of careers,” he cried. “Yes, madame, learn, if you do not know it, that a great artist is a king, and more than a king; he is happier, independent, he lives as he likes, he reigns in the world of fancy. Your son has a glorious future before him. Faculties like his are rare; they are only disclosed at his age in such beings as Giottos, Raphaels, Titians, Rubens, Murillos—for, in my opinion, he will make a better painter than sculptor. God of heaven! if I had such a son, I should be as happy as the Emperor is to have given himself the King of Rome. Well, you are mistress of your child’s fate. Go your own way, madame; make him a fool, a miserable quill-driver, tie him to a desk, and you’ve murdered him! But I hope, in spite of all your efforts, that he will stay an artist. A true vocation is stronger than obstacles. Vocation! the very word means a call, the election of God himself! You will make your child unhappy, that’s all.” He flung the clay he no longer needed into a tub and said to his model, “That will do for to-day.”

Agathe raised her eyes and saw, in a corner where her glance had not before penetrated, a nude woman sitting on a stool; the sight drove her away horrified.

“You are not to have the little Bridau here any more,” Chaudet said to pupils, “it annoys his mother.”

“Eugh!” they cried as Agathe closed the door.

No sooner did the students learn Madame Bridau did not wish her son to be an artist than their whole happiness centered on getting Joseph among them. In spite of a promise not to go to the Institute, the child often slipped into Regnauld the painter’s studio, where he was encouraged to daub canvas. When the widow complained the bargain was not kept, Chaudet’s pupils assured her Regnauld was not Chaudet and they hadn’t the bringing up of her son, with other impertinences; the scamps composed a song of a hundred and thirty-seven couplets on Madame Bridau.

That evening Agathe refused to play at cards and sat on her sofa plunged in grief, tears in her handsome eyes.

“What is the matter, Madame Bridau?” asked old Claparon.

“She thinks her boy will have to beg his bread because he has got the bump of painting,” said Madame Descoings; “but I am not uneasy about the future of my step-son, little Bixiou, who has a passion for drawing. Men are born to get on.”

“You are right,” said the severe Desroches, who, despite talent, had never risen beyond assistant-head of a department. “Happily I have only one son; otherwise, with my eighteen hundred francs, and a wife who makes barely twelve hundred from her stamped-paper office, I don’t know what would become of me. I have just placed my boy as under-clerk to a lawyer; he gets twenty-five francs a month and his breakfast. I give him as much more, and he dines and sleeps at home. That’s all he gets; he must manage for himself, but he’ll make his way. I keep the fellow harder at work than if he were at school, and some day he will be a barrister. When I give him money to go to the theatre, he is as happy as a king and kisses me. Oh, I keep a tight hand on him, and he renders me an account of all he spends. You are too good to your children, Madame Bridau; if your son wants hardships and privations, let him; they’ll make a man of him.”

“As for my boy,” said Du Bruel, a retired chief of a division, “he is only sixteen; his mother dotes on him; but I wouldn’t listen to his choosing a profession at his age—a mere fancy that may pass. Boys should be guided and controlled.”

“Ah, monsieur! you are rich, you are a man, and you have but one son,” said Agathe.

“Faith!” said Claparon, “children do tyrannize over us—over our hearts, I mean. Mine makes me furious; he has nearly ruined me, and now I won’t have anything to do with him—it’s a sort of independence. Well, he is the happier for it, and so am I. That fellow was partly the cause of his mother’s death. He chose to be a commercial traveller; and the trade suited him, for he was no sooner in the house than he wanted to be out; he couldn’t keep in one place and wouldn’t learn. All I ask of God is that I may die before he dishonors my name. Those without children lose many pleasures, but they escape great sufferings.”

“And these men are fathers!” thought Agathe, weeping anew.

“What I am trying to show you, my dear Madame Bridau, is that you had better let your boy be a painter; if not, you will only waste your time.”

“If you could coerce him,” said the sour Desroches, “I should advise you to oppose his tastes; but weak as I see you are, you had better let him daub if he likes.”

“Console yourself, Agathe,” said Madame Descoings, “Joseph will turn out a great man.”

After this discussion the widow’s friends united in one advice, which did not relieve her anxieties. They advised her to let Joseph follow his bent.

“If he doesn’t turn out a genius,” said Du Bruel, who always sought to please Agathe, “you can then get him into some government office.”

When Madame Descoings accompanied the old clerks to the door she assured them at the head of the stairs that they were “Grecian sages.”

“Madame Bridau ought to be glad her son is willing to do anything,” said Claparon.

“Besides,” said Desroches, “if God preserves the Emperor, Joseph will always be looked after. Why should she worry?”

“She is timid about everything concerning her children,” answered Madame Descoings. “Well, my good girl,” she said returning to Agathe, “you see they are unanimous; why are you still crying?”

“If it was Philippe, I should have no anxiety. But you don’t know what goes on in that atelier; they have naked women!”

“I hope they keep good fires,” said Madame Descoings.

A few days after this, the disasters of the retreat from Moscow became known. Napoleon returned to Paris to organize fresh troops and to ask further sacrifices from the country. The poor mother was then plunged into different anxieties. Philippe, tired of school, wanted to serve the Emperor; he saw a review at the Tuileries—the last Napoleon ever held—and became infatuated with a soldier’s life. In those days military splendor, the show of uniforms, the authority of epaulets, offered irresistible seductions to a certain style of youth. Philippe thought he had the same vocation for the army that his brother showed for art. Without his mother’s knowledge he wrote a petition to the Emperor:

Sire,—I am the son of your Bridau; eighteen years of age, five feet six inches; I have good legs, a good constitution, and wish to be one of your soldiers. I ask you to let me enter the army, etc.

Within twenty-four hours the Emperor had sent Philippe to the Imperial Lyceum at Saint-Cyr, and six months later, in November 1813, appointed him sub-lieutenant in a cavalry regiment. Philippe spent most of that winter in cantonments, but as soon as he knew to ride he was dispatched to the front and went eagerly. During the campaign in France he was made a lieutenant after an outpost affair where his bravery saved his colonel’s life. The Emperor named him captain at La Fere-Champenoise and took him on his staff. Inspired by such promotion, Philippe won the cross at Montereau. He witnessed Napoleon’s farewell at Fontainebleau, raved at the sight, and refused to serve the Bourbons. When he returned in July 1814, he found his mother ruined.

Joseph’s scholarship was withdrawn after the holidays, and Madame Bridau, whose pension came from the Emperor’s privy purse, vainly entreated that it be inscribed on the ministry rolls. Joseph, more of a painter than ever, was delighted and entreated his mother to let him go to Monsieur Regnauld, promising to earn his own living. He declared he was sufficiently advanced in the second class to get on without rhetoric. Philippe, a captain at nineteen and decorated, who had served the Emperor as aide-de-camp in two battles, flattered the mother’s vanity immensely. Coarse and blustering, without real merit beyond cavalry bravery, he was to her a man of genius; whereas Joseph, puny and sickly, with unkempt hair and absent mind, seeking peace and dreaming of an artist’s glory, would only bring her worries.

The winter of 1814-1815 was lucky for Joseph. Secretly encouraged by Madame Descoings and Bixiou, a pupil of Gros, he worked in that celebrated atelier, whence much talent issued, and there formed a close intimacy with Schinner. The return from Elba came; Captain Bridau joined the Emperor at Lyons, accompanied him to the Tuileries, and was appointed to command a squadron in the Guard’s dragoons. After Waterloo—where he was slightly wounded and won the officer’s Legion of Honor—he happened to be near Marshal Davoust at Saint-Denis and was not with the Loire army. Consequently, and through Davoust’s intercession, his cross and rank were secured though he was placed on half-pay.

Joseph, anxious about his future, studied through this period with an ardor that several times made him ill amid these tumultuous events.

“It is the smell of the paints,” Agathe said to Madame Descoings. “He ought to give up a business so injurious to his health.”

However, all Agathe’s anxieties were for her lieutenant-colonel son. When she saw him again in 1816, reduced from a nine-thousand-franc commander’s salary to a half-pay of three hundred francs a month, she fitted up attic rooms for him and spent her savings. Philippe was one of the faithful Bonapartes of the cafe Lemblin, that constitutional Boeotia; he acquired habits, manners, style, and life of a half-pay officer; like any young man of twenty-one he exaggerated them, vowed mortal enmity to the Bourbons, never reported to the War Department, and refused opportunities offered for infantry employ with his rank. In his mother’s eyes, Philippe seemed to display noble character.

“The father himself could have done no more,” she said.

Philippe’s half-pay sufficed him; he cost nothing at home, whereas all Joseph’s expenses were paid by the two widows. From that moment Agathe’s preference for Philippe was openly shown. Up to that time it had been secret; but the persecution of this faithful servant of the Emperor, the recollection of his wound, his courage in adversity—which, voluntary though it was, seemed a glorious adversity—drew forth Agathe’s tenderness. The single sentence “He is unfortunate” explained and justified everything. Joseph himself—with the simplicity abundant in the artist-soul in its opening years, and brought up to admire his big brother—so far from being hurt by maternal preference, encouraged it by sharing her worship of the hero who had carried Napoleon’s orders on two battlefields and was wounded at Waterloo. How could he doubt the superiority of the grand brother whom he had seen in the green and gold uniform of the Guard’s dragoons commanding his squadron on the Champ de Mars?

Agathe, notwithstanding this preference, was an excellent mother. She loved Joseph, though not blindly; she simply could not understand him. Joseph adored his mother; Philippe let his mother adore him. Towards her the dragoon softened his military brutality, but he never concealed the contempt he felt for Joseph—expressed, however, in a friendly way. When he looked at his brother, weak and sickly at seventeen, shrunken by toil and over-weighted with his powerful head, he nicknamed him “Cub.” Philippe’s patronizing manners would have wounded anyone less carelessly indifferent than the artist, who believed soldiers hid goodness beneath a brutal exterior. Joseph did not yet know that soldiers of genius are as gentle and courteous as other superior men. All genius is alike.

“Poor boy!” said Philippe to his mother, “we mustn’t plague him; let him do as he likes.”

To his mother’s eyes the colonel’s contempt was a mark of fraternal affection.

“Philippe will always love and protect his brother,” she thought.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In 1816 Joseph obtained his mother’s permission to convert the garret next to his attic room into an atelier; Madame Descoings gave a little money for the painter’s necessities. The two widows regarded painting as a mere trade, but Joseph, with the ardor of his vocation, arranged the humble studio himself. Madame Descoings persuaded the owner to put a skylight in the roof; Joseph painted the hall a chocolate color and hung a few sketches. Agathe reluctantly added an iron stove so her son might work at home without abandoning the studios of Gros or Schinner.

The constitutional party, backed largely by half-pay officers and Bonapartists, was stirring “emeutes” around the Chamber of Deputies; conspiracies were rumored. Philippe, dabbling in them, was arrested and released for lack of proof, though the minister of war put him back on the active list, cutting short his half-pay. France was unsafe; Agathe feared provocateurs and traps. Philippe passed time in disaffected cafés, drinking and acquiring bad habits, while Agathe lived in mortal terror for him. The Grecian sages still called nightly at Madame Bridau’s. Claparon, retained at the ministry though cautious, whispered news of the Moniteur. Desroches, retired after old Du Bruel, was still fighting for his pension. Their friends advised Agathe to send Philippe abroad.

“They talk of conspiracies, and your son will be certain to fall a victim,” they warned.

“Philippe is cut from the wood the Emperor made into marshals,” said Du Bruel; “he mustn’t give up his profession. Let him serve in the East, in India—”

“Think of his health,” Agathe replied.

“Why doesn’t he get some place, some business?” asked Desroches. “There are private offices to be had.” Agathe, warlike, insisted Philippe was a soldier and wouldn’t want anything else. Du Bruel suggested royal favor could appoint him lieutenant-colonel to a regiment, though Madame Descoings doubted his prospects without noble birth.

To please his mother, Philippe took up the notion of joining General Lallemand in the United States and helping found the Champ d’Asile, one of the most disastrous swindles under the name of a national subscription. Agathe gave ten thousand francs and saw him off at Havre. By the end of 1817 she was living on the six hundred francs a year left from her funds and, by good investment, secured seven percent on some savings. Joseph emulated her frugality: dressed plainly, denied himself comforts, and lived on bread, milk, and Brie; only Madame Descoings and Bixiou showed sympathy.

Joseph later recalled how the coming summer saved him the cost of charcoal. Now a colorist as capable as Gros, Joseph consulted his master only occasionally. He was already planning a revolt against classical traditions, aiming to free art to represent Nature. He prepared for a struggle that began with his first Salon exhibition and never ceased.

1818 was terrible for the family. Roguin, Madame Descoings’s notary, absconded with seven years’ withheld annuity funds. Three days later a bill of exchange for a thousand francs arrived from New York: Philippe had lost everything in the Champ d’Asile. The letter told of debts contracted and of comrades who had indorsed for him. Agathe, blaming herself, wept. The two women faced selling silver, linen, and furniture. Joseph, alarmed, told Gérard, who secured an order for two royal copies of Louis XVIII’s portrait at five hundred francs each and fitted Joseph out with materials. Joseph painted four panels in ten days, sold them, and brought his mother the thousand francs to meet Philippe’s draft. Then a letter announced Philippe’s imminent return on a packet whose captain had trusted him for passage; he would need a thousand francs at Havre.

Joseph promised his mother the money after finishing the copies. Agathe, ecstatically grateful, pressed Joseph for a kiss and called him “his father all over.” Madame Bridau rushed to Havre and watched beyond the round tower every day for the packet. The sight of home softened Philippe. But his misfortunes in Texas and New York had developed the worst traits in him: he had grown brutal, selfish, rude, drank and smoked excessively, and conceived himself perpetually persecuted. Such men either live by honest virtues or by treating every want and impulse as necessity; Philippe had drifted into the latter, becoming cunning and self-centered while keeping an appearance of frank soldierly simplicity. His stature had become somewhat rotund, his face bronzed, his speech terse and decisive; exposed to hardship, he had become the kind of man who makes himself feared. To his mother he remained a hero; in truth he had become, bluntly, a blackguard.

At Havre Agathe bought him clothes and could not, initially, restrain his indulgence. The scheme of conquering Texas with imperial remnants had failed less for lack of idea than for treachery and poor management; the Liberals had used the enterprise selfishly, and abandoned the devoted heroes. Agathe, a household manager with shrewd common sense, saw through the political chicane and recognized the subscription’s failure; the poor woman could not make her son see he was duped rather than persecuted. During the costly week at Havre she could not persuade him to make terms with the royal government. She brought him back to Paris before her funds gave out.

Upon arrival, those awaiting them noted how much Agathe had aged. Philippe greeted Madame Descoings coolly. Joseph, now twenty-one and confident, was hurt by his brother’s contempt. At a family dinner the friends, including Du Bruel, Claparon, Desroches, and the Abbe Loraux, observed the returned exile’s roughened manners and coarse voice. While Joseph prepared the card tables, the intimate friends asked Agathe what she intended for Philippe. She could not get him to serve the Bourbons; Du Bruel suggested alternatives like writing plays as his son had done. The exile, Bixiou, and young Desroches played ecarte. Madame Descoings, eager to make a festival of the prodigal’s return, provided funds for play; Joseph secretly slipped twenty francs to Philippe.

The evening grew lively. Philippe, a poor player, ended owing fifty francs. He showed bad temper and quarreled with young Desroches and then with Desroches senior, who, though his son was right, forbade further play. Madame Descoings stopped her grandson’s barbed witticisms for fear of provoking the colonel. Philippe’s bad temper and gambling continued into the night.

The next morning the two widows, counting resources, decided they must economize: Madame Descoings proposed selling furniture and giving up apartments to save seven hundred francs a year and pay Philippe fifty francs monthly until he found something. They pooled incomes—about five thousand three hundred francs, four thousand from Madame Descoings as a life annuity—and had consumed their savings. Philippe promised to find a situation and asked only for board and lodging. Agathe kissed him; Madame Descoings slipped him a hundred francs to cover his losses.

In ten days the furniture was sold and the household rearranged. Philippe’s habits, however, hardened: he decamped after breakfast, returned at dinner, and spent evenings in cafés, drinking brandy while reading newspapers, sauntering to the café Minerve to play billiards and drink more, smoking many cigars, and gambling at night. He boasted of a regular life and, while often winning, returned home talking of smashing the ultras and the Bourbon body-guard.

He dined often with army comrades who spoke freely of hopes based on plots and inventions like a submarine that would restore the Emperor. He particularly liked an old dragoons’ captain, Giroudeau, whose company led Philippe into further dissipation and into seeing women as resources. Giroudeau, though a bluff old roue, introduced Philippe to the theatre and to a young dancer, Florentine, and to another, Marie Godeschal—Mariette—who was beautiful, cold, and ambitious. Mariette, orphaned and poor, had a devoted brother who clerked in Derville’s law office; she resolved to use her beauty and talent to help him. Poorly dressed yet striking, she provoked Philippe’s passion. To Mariette, Philippe was a Guard staff-officer, and a match with him would elevate her above rivals. Florentine and Giroudeau encouraged a kind of morganatic liaison between Philippe and Mariette.

Giroudeau promised to speak to his nephew Finot at a theatrical journal and to get Philippe a place on a newspaper. Philippe, revealed his poverty, but Giroudeau reassured him: his nephew could put him on a paper or help him get patronage. Mariette, thinking Philippe would write for a newspaper and help her career, accepted him.

The next day Philippe met Giroudeau at the subscription office where the veteran lived in a cramped cage-like room labeled “Subscription Office.” There they encountered Coloquinte, a one-armed man, and then climbed to Giroudeau’s nephew Finot’s attic, where Finot, cool and sardonic, eyed Philippe. Giroudeau praised the colonel; Finot mocked the Texan affair a little, then gave Philippe a plan: exploit his position as a victim of the Liberals and demand an account of the Champ d’Asile subscription. A new Opposition journal was starting; Finot could make Philippe its cashier with a salary of three thousand francs if Philippe found someone to go security for twenty thousand francs. “Threaten loudly,” Finot advised, “and we’ll silence you with a place.” Philippe was grateful and impressed by Finot’s worldly cleverness.

That night at cafés Philippe fulminated against the Liberals, declaring he would demand an accounting of the moneys collected. He went to Mariette to tell her of the expected post on a newspaper with ten thousand subscribers and of how he would advance her claims.

Agathe and Madame Descoings waited anxiously for Philippe one night amid the political alarm following the assassination of the Duc de Berry. He returned with good news: he had been promised the cashiership of a new journal at three thousand francs, on condition they could go security for twenty thousand francs, by depositing their shares in the Funds as guarantee while drawing the interest. The friends urged Agathe to go security; she and Madame Descoings, overjoyed at the prospect of providing for Philippe, transferred her shares—representing thirteen hundred francs in interest—to secure him. Philippe promised to pay one hundred francs monthly for board and lodging and was acclaimed the best of sons. Those who had doubted him congratulated Agathe and admitted they had been unjust. Joseph, not to be outdone, resolved to pay for his own support and succeeded.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER IV

Three months later the colonel, who ate and drank enough for four, found fault with the food and compelled the poor widows, on the ground of his pay, to spend much on their table, though he had not yet paid a penny. His mother and Madame Descoings, out of delicacy, would not remind him of his promise. The year passed without one of those coins Leon Gozlan so vigorously called “tigers with five claws” finding its way from Philippe’s pocket to the household purse. He quieted his conscience by seldom dining at home.

“Well, he is happy,” said his mother; “he is easy in mind; he has a place.”

Through the influence of a feuilleton edited by Vernou, a friend of Bixiou, Finot, and Giroudeau, Mariette appeared not at the Panorama-Dramatique but at the Porte-Saint-Martin, where she triumphed beside the famous Begrand. Among the directors was a rich and luxurious general in love with an actress, for whose sake he had become an impresario. This officer knew Philippe and Giroudeau. Mariette’s first appearance, heralded by Finot’s journal and by Philippe, was promptly arranged by the three officers; there seems to be solidarity among the passions in folly.

The mischievous Bixiou soon revealed to his grandmother and the devoted Agathe that Philippe, the cashier, the hero of heroes, was in love with Mariette, the celebrated ballet-dancer. The news was a thunder-clap to the two widows; Agathe’s religious principles taught her that women on the stage were brands in the burning; she and Madame Descoings thought such women dined off gold, drank pearls, and wasted fortunes.

“Now do you suppose,” said Joseph to his mother, “that my brother is such a fool as to spend his money on Mariette? Such women only ruin rich men.”

“They talk of engaging Mariette at the Opera,” said Bixiou. “Don’t be worried, Madame Bridau; the diplomatic body often comes to the Porte-Saint-Martin, and that handsome girl won’t stay long with your son. I heard an ambassador was madly in love with her. By the bye, another piece of news! Old Claparon is dead, and his son, who has become a banker, has ordered the cheapest kind of funeral for him. That fellow has no education; they wouldn’t behave like that in China.”

Philippe, prompted by mercenary motives, proposed marriage to Mariette; but she, knowing herself on the eve of an engagement at the Grand Opera, refused, either because she guessed the colonel’s motive or because she prized independence for her fortune. For the remainder of the year Philippe came home no more than twice a month. Where was he? Either at his office, the theatre, or with Mariette. No light as to his conduct reached the household of the rue Mazarin. Giroudeau, Finot, Bixiou, Vernou, Lousteau saw him leading a life of pleasure. Philippe shared the gay amusements of Tullia, a leading singer at the Opera, of Florentine, who took Mariette’s place at the Porte-Saint-Martin, of Florine and Matifat, Coralie and Camusot. After four o’clock, when he left his office, until midnight, he amused himself; some party had usually been arranged the night before—a good dinner, a card-party, a supper by some one of the set. Philippe was in his element.

This carnival, which lasted eighteen months, was not altogether without troubles. The beautiful Mariette no sooner appeared at the Opera in January, 1821, than she captured one of the most distinguished dukes of Louis XVIII’s court. Philippe tried to make head against the peer, and by April, compelled by his passion and despite some luck at cards, he dipped into the funds he was cashier of. By May he had taken eleven hundred francs. In that fatal month Mariette started for London to see what could be done with the lords while the temporary opera house in the Hotel Choiseul, rue Lepelletier, was being prepared. The luckless Philippe had ended, as often happens, in loving Mariette despite her infidelities; she herself had never thought him anything but a dull-minded, brutal soldier, the first rung of a ladder she had never intended to remain on. Foreseeing the time when Philippe would have spent all his money, she captured other journalistic support which released her from depending on him; nevertheless, she felt the peculiar gratitude that that class of women acknowledge towards the first man who smooths their way among the difficulties and horrors of a theatrical career.

Forced to let his terrible mistress go to London without him, Philippe went into winter quarters—returned to his attic room in his mother’s apartment. He made gloomy reflections as he went to bed and when he rose. He was conscious of the inability to live otherwise than he had for the last year. The luxury that surrounded Mariette—the dinners, the suppers, the evenings in the side-scenes, the animation of wits and journalists, the racket around him, the delights that tickled his senses and vanity—such a life, found only in Paris, offering daily the charm of something new, was now more than habit—it had become to Philippe as necessary as his tobacco or brandy. He saw plainly that he could not live without these continual enjoyments. The idea of suicide came into his head; not on account of the deficit which must soon be discovered, but because he could no longer live without Mariette’s atmosphere of pleasure. Full of gloomy thoughts, he entered his brother’s painting-room for the first time, where he found Joseph in a blue blouse, copying a picture for a dealer.

“So that’s how pictures are made,” said Philippe, by way of opening.

“No,” said Joseph, “that is how they are copied.”

“How much do they pay you for that?”

“Eh! never enough; two hundred and fifty francs. But I study the manner of the masters and learn much; I found out the secrets of their method. There’s one of my own pictures,” he added, pointing to a sketch with colors still moist.

“How much do you pocket in a year?”

“Unfortunately, I am known only to painters. Schinner backs me; he has got me work at the Chateau de Presles, where I am going in October to do arabesques, panels, and other decorations, for which the Comte de Serizy, no doubt, will pay well. With such trifles and with orders from the dealers, I may manage to earn eighteen hundred to two thousand francs a year over and above expenses. I shall send that picture to the next exhibition; if it hits the public taste, my fortune is made. My friends think well of it.”

“I don’t know anything about such things,” said Philippe in a subdued voice, causing Joseph to look at him.

“What is the matter?” the artist asked, seeing Philippe very pale.

“I should like to know how long it would take you to paint my portrait.”

“If I worked steadily, and the weather were clear, I could finish in three or four days.”

“That’s too long; I have only one day. My poor mother loves me so I wished to leave her my likeness. We will say no more.”

“Why! are you going away again?”

“I am going never to return,” replied Philippe with forced gaiety.

“Look here, Philippe, what is the matter? If it is anything serious, I am a man and not a ninny. I am used to hard struggles, and if discretion is needed, I have it.”

“Are you sure?”

“On my honor.”

“You will tell no one, no matter who?”

“No one.”

“Well, I am going to blow my brains out.”

“You!—are you going to fight a duel?”

“I am going to kill myself.”

“Why?”

“I have taken eleven hundred francs from the funds in my hands; I must send in my accounts tomorrow. Half my security is lost; our poor mother will be reduced to six hundred francs a year. That would be nothing! I could make a fortune for her later; but I am dishonored! I cannot live under dishonor—”

“You will not be dishonored if it is paid back. To be sure, you will lose your place and only have the five hundred francs a year from your cross, but you can live on five hundred francs.”

“Farewell!” said Philippe, running downstairs and not waiting for another word.

Joseph left his studio and went down to breakfast with his mother; Philippe’s confession had taken away his appetite. He took Madame Descoings aside and told her the terrible news. The old woman made a frightened exclamation, let fall the saucepan of milk she held, and flung herself into a chair. Agathe rushed in; from one exclamation to another the mother gathered the fatal truth.

“He! to fail in honor! the son of Bridau to take the money that was trusted to him!”

The widow trembled; her eyes dilated and then grew fixed; she sat down and burst into tears.

“Where is he?” she cried amid sobs. “Perhaps he has flung himself into the Seine.”

“You must not give up hope,” said Madame Descoings, “because a poor lad has met a bad woman who led him to do wrong. Dear me! we see that every day. Philippe has had such misfortunes! he had so little chance to be happy and loved that we ought not to be surprised at his passion for that creature. All passions lead to excess. My own life is not without reproach of that kind, and yet I call myself an honest woman. A single fault is not vice; and after all, it is only those who do nothing that are never deceived.”

Agathe’s despair overcame her so much that Joseph and the Descoings had to lessen Philippe’s wrongdoing by assuring her such things happened in all families.

“But he is twenty-eight years old,” cried Agathe, “he is no longer a child.”

Terrible revelation of the poor woman’s inward thought about her son’s conduct.

“Mother, I assure you he thought only of your sufferings and of the wrong he had done you,” said Joseph.

“Oh, my God! let him come back to me, let him live, and I will forgive all,” cried the poor mother, who pictured Philippe dragged dead out of the river.

Gloomy silence reigned a short time. The day passed with cruel alternations of hope and fear; all three ran to the window at the least sound and gave way to every sort of conjecture. While the family grieved, Philippe was quietly getting matters in order at his office. He had the audacity to submit accounts with a statement that, fearing some accident, he had retained eleven hundred francs at his house for safe keeping. The scoundrel left the office at five, taking five hundred francs more from the desk, and coolly went to a gambling-house he had avoided since his paper connection, for a cashier must not be seen frequenting such places. The fellow was not wanting in acumen. His past conduct showed he derived more from his grandfather Rouget than from his virtuous sire Bridau. Perhaps he might have made a good general; but in private life he was one of those scoundrels who shelter their schemes behind a screen of strict legality and the privacy of the family roof.

At this conjuncture Philippe maintained his coolness. He won at first and gained as much as six thousand francs; but he let himself be dazzled by the idea of getting out of his difficulties at one stroke. He left the trente-et-quarante, hearing that the black had come up sixteen times at roulette, and was about to put five thousand francs on the red when the black came for the seventeenth time. The colonel then put a thousand francs on the black and won. In spite of this luck, his head grew weary; he felt it, though he continued to play. But that divining sense which leads a gambler comes in flashes and began failing him. Intermittent perceptions, fatal to gamblers, set in. Lucidity of mind, like the rays of the sun, can have no effect except by continuity; it can divine only if not breaking that line; the curvettings of chance bemuddle it. Philippe lost all. After such a strain, the careless mind as well as the bravest weakens. When Philippe went home that night he was not thinking of suicide; he had never really meant to kill himself; he no longer thought of his lost place, nor of the sacrificed security, nor of his mother, nor of Mariette, the cause of his ruin; he walked along mechanically. When he got home, his mother in tears, Madame Descoings and Joseph all fell on his neck and kissed him and brought him to a seat by the fire.

“Bless me!” thought he, “the threat has worked.”

The brute at once assumed an air suitable to the occasion; the more easily because his ill-luck at cards had deeply depressed him. Seeing her atrocious Benjamin so pale and woe-begone, the poor mother knelt beside him, kissed his hands, pressed them to her heart, and gazed at him for a long time with eyes swimming in tears.

“Philippe,” she said in a choking voice, “promise not to kill yourself, and all shall be forgotten.”

Philippe looked at his sorrowing brother and at Madame Descoings, whose eyes were full of tears, and thought, “They are good creatures.” Then he took his mother in his arms, raised her and put her on his knee, pressed her to his heart and whispered as he kissed her, “For the second time, you give me life.”

The Descoings managed to serve an excellent dinner and add two bottles of old wine with a little “liqueur des iles,” a treasure left from her former business.

“Agathe,” she said at dessert, “we must let him smoke his cigars,” and she offered some to Philippe.

These two poor creatures fancied that if they let him take his ease he would like his home and stay; both tried to endure his tobacco-smoke, though each loathed it. That sacrifice was not so much noticed by Philippe.

On the morrow Agathe looked ten years older. Her terrors calmed, reflection returned, and the poor woman had not closed an eye through that horrible night. She was now reduced to six hundred francs a year. Madame Descoings, like all fat women fond of good eating, was growing heavy; her step on the staircase sounded like chopping logs; she might die at any moment; with her life, four thousand francs would disappear. What folly to rely on that resource! What should she do? What would become of them? With her mind made up to become a sick-nurse rather than be supported by her children, Agathe did not think of herself. But Philippe—what would he do if reduced to live on the five hundred francs of a Legion of Honor officer? During the past eleven years Madame Descoings, by giving up three thousand francs a year, had paid her debt twice, yet she still sacrificed her grandson’s interests to the Bridau family. Though Agathe’s honorable feelings were shocked by this disaster, she said to herself: “Poor boy! is it his fault? He is faithful to his oath. I have done wrong not to marry him. If I had found him a wife, he would not have entangled with this danseuse. He has such a vigorous constitution—”

Madame Descoings had likewise reflected on saving the family honor. At daybreak she went to her friend’s room.

“Neither you nor Philippe should manage this delicate matter,” she urged. “Our two old friends Du Bruel and Claparon are dead, but we still have Desroches, who is very sagacious. I’ll go see him this morning. He can tell the newspaper people that Philippe trusted a friend and has been made a victim; that his weakness makes him unfit to be a cashier; what happened may happen again, and that Philippe prefers to resign. That will prevent his being turned off.”

Agathe, seeing this lie would save her son’s honor in others’ eyes, kissed Madame Descoings, who went out early to finish the dreadful affair.

Philippe, meanwhile, had slept the sleep of the just. “She is sly, that old woman,” he remarked when his mother explained why breakfast was late.

Old Desroches, the last remaining friend of these two poor women, who despite his harsh nature never forgot that Bridau had obtained his place, fulfilled like a diplomat the mission Madame Descoings had confided to him. He dined that evening with the family and notified Agathe she must go the next day to the Treasury, rue Vivienne, sign the transfer of funds involved, and obtain a coupon for the six hundred francs a year that remained to her. The old clerk did not leave without obliging Philippe to sign a petition to the minister of war asking for reinstatement in the active army. Desroches promised to follow the petition at the war office, and to profit by the triumph of a duke over Philippe in the matter of the danseuse to obtain that nobleman’s influence.

“Philippe will be lieutenant-colonel in the Duc de Maufrigneuse’s regiment within three months,” he declared, “and you will be rid of him.”

Desroches went away smothered with blessings from the two poor widows and Joseph. As to the newspaper, it ceased to exist in two months, just as Finot predicted. Philippe’s crime had, so far as the world knew, no consequences. But Agathe’s motherhood had received a deadly wound. Her belief in her son once shaken, she lived in perpetual fear, mingled with some satisfactions as she saw her worst apprehensions unrealized.

When men like Philippe, endowed with physical courage yet cowardly and ignoble morally, see things resume their course after a catastrophe in which honor is almost lost, family kindness or friendliness is a premium of encouragement. They count on impunity; their minds distorted, their passions gratified, they study how they succeeded in getting round social laws; the result is they become alarmingly adroit.

A fortnight later Philippe, once more a man of leisure, lazy and bored, renewed his fatal café life—drams, long billiard games with punch, nightly resort to the gambling-table where he risked trifling stakes and won enough to pay his dissipations. Apparently very economical, the better to deceive his mother and Madame Descoings, he wore a greasy hat with the nap rubbed off, patched boots, a shabby overcoat on which the red ribbon scarcely showed so discolored and dirty, his buckskin gloves greenish, and he gave up his satin neckcloth only when ragged enough to look like wadding. Mariette was the sole object of his love, and her treachery had greatly hardened his heart. When he won more than usual, or supped with Giroudeau, he followed some Venus of the slums with brutal contempt for the whole sex. Otherwise regular in his habits, he breakfasted and dined at home and came in about one o’clock. Three months of this life restored Agathe to some confidence.

As for Joseph, working at the splendid picture that later brought him fame, he lived in his atelier. On Bixiou’s prediction, Madame Descoings believed in Joseph’s future glory and showed him every motherly kindness; she took his breakfast, ran errands, blacked his boots. The painter was never seen till dinner-time, and his evenings were spent at the Cenacle among friends. He read much and gave himself the deep education that comes through the mind itself, which men of talent seek between twenty and thirty. Agathe, seeing little of Joseph and feeling no uneasiness, lived only for Philippe, who gave her alternations of fear and relief necessary to maternity. Desroches, who came once a week, gave her hopes. The Duc de Maufrigneuse had asked to have Philippe in his regiment; the minister of war had ordered an inquiry; and as Bridau’s name did not appear on any police list nor at the Palais de Justice, Philippe would be reinstated early in the coming year.

To this result Desroches set all his influence in motion. At the prefecture of police he learned Philippe spent his evenings gambling and thought best to tell this privately to Madame Descoings, exhorting her to keep an eye on the lieutenant-colonel, for one outbreak would imperil all; as it was, the minister of war was not likely to inquire whether Philippe gambled. Once restored under the flag, he might abandon a vice taken up from idleness. Agathe, who no longer received friends, sat in the chimney-corner reading her prayers, while Madame Descoings consulted the cards, interpreted dreams, and applied the rules of the “cabala” to her lottery ventures. This jovial fanatic never missed a drawing; she still pursued her trey—which never turned up. It was nearly twenty-one years old, just approaching majority; on this ridiculous idea she pinned her faith. One of its three numbers had stayed at the bottom of all wheels since the lottery’s institution. Accordingly Madame Descoings laid heavy stakes on that number and on all combinations of the three numbers. The last mattress of her bed was where she stored savings; she unsewed the ticking, put in bits of gold saved from her needs, wrapped them in wool, and sewed the mattress up again. She intended, at the last drawing, to risk all her savings on the different combinations of her treasured trey.

This passion, so universally condemned, has never been fairly studied. No one has understood this opium of poverty. The lottery, all-powerful fairy of the poor, bestowed the gift of magic hopes. The turn of the wheel which opens to the gambler a vista of gold and happiness lasts no longer than a flash, but the lottery gave five days’ existence to that flash. What social power today, for five sous, gives us five days’ happiness and launches us ideally into all the joys of civilization? Tobacco, a craving far more immoral than play, destroys the body, attacks the mind, and stupefies a nation; while the lottery did none of that. This passion was forced to keep within limits by the long periods between drawings and by the choice of wheels each investor clung to. Madame Descoings never staked on any but the “wheel of Paris.” Full of confidence that the trey cherished for twenty-one years was about to triumph, she imposed enormous privations to stake a large sum on the last drawing. When she dreamed cabalistic visions (not all corresponding to lottery numbers), she told Joseph, who alone would listen and not scold her but soothe her follies with kindly words. All great talents respect and understand a real passion; they explain it to themselves by finding its roots in their own hearts. Joseph’s idea was that his brother loved tobacco and liquors, Maman Descoings loved her trey, his mother loved God, Desroches the younger loved lawsuits, Desroches the elder loved angling—in short, all the world loved something. He himself loved the “beau idéal” in all things; he loved Byron’s poetry, Gericault’s painting, Rossini’s music, Walter Scott’s novels. “Everyone to his taste, maman,” he would say; “but your trey does hang fire terribly.”

“It will turn up, and you will be rich, and my little Bixiou as well.”

“Give it all to your grandson,” cried Joseph; “at any rate, do what you like with it.”

“Hey! when it turns up I shall have enough for everybody. In the first place, you shall have a fine atelier; you sha’n’t deprive yourself of going to the opera so as to pay for your models and colors. Do you know, my dear boy, you make me play a pretty shabby part in that picture of yours?”

By way of economy, Joseph had made the Descoings pose for his magnificent painting of a young courtesan taken by an old woman to a Doge of Venice. This picture, one of the masterpieces of modern painting, was mistaken by Gros himself for a Titian, and paved the way for recognition Joseph received in the Salon of 1823.

“Those who know you know very well what you are,” he answered gayly. “Why need you trouble about those who don’t know you?”

For the last ten years Madame Descoings had taken on the ripe tints of a russet apple at Easter. Wrinkles had formed in her superabundant flesh, now pallid and flabby. Her eyes, full of life, were bright with thoughts still young and vivacious, and might be considered grasping; there is always something of that spirit in a gambler.
