“Hey!” cried Joseph, “there’s a fine woman, and a rare one! She’s made, as they say, to paint. What flesh-tints! Oh, the lovely tones! what surface! what curves! Ah, those shoulders! She’s a magnificent caryatid. What a model she would have been for one of Titian’s Venuses!”

Adolphine and Madame Hochon thought him talking Greek; Agathe signed behind his back that she was used to such jargon.

“So you think a creature who is depriving you of your property handsome?” said Madame Hochon.

“That doesn’t prevent her from being a splendid model—just plump enough not to spoil the hips and contour—”

“My son, you are not in your studio,” said Agathe. “Adolphine is here.”

“Ah, true! I did wrong. But you must remember that since leaving Paris I’ve seen nothing but ugly women—”

“My dear godmother,” Agathe said hastily, “how shall I meet my brother if that creature is always with him?”

“Bah!” said Joseph. “I’ll go and see him myself. I don’t think him such an idiot now I find he has the sense to rejoice his eyes with a Titian’s Venus.”

“If he were not an idiot,” said Monsieur Hochon, who had come in, “he would have married long ago and had children; and then you would have no chance at the property. It is an ill wind that blows no good.”

“Your son’s idea is very good,” said Madame Hochon; “he ought to pay the first visit. He can make his uncle understand that if you call there he must be alone.”

“That will affront Mademoiselle Brazier,” said old Hochon. “No, no, madame; swallow the pill. If you can’t get the whole property, secure a small legacy.”

The Hochons were not clever enough to match Max. In the middle of breakfast Kouski brought a letter from Monsieur Rouget to his sister, Madame Bridau. Madame Hochon made her husband read it aloud:

My dear Sister,—I learn from strangers of your arrival in Issoudun. I can guess the reason which made you prefer Monsieur and Madame Hochon’s house to mine; but if you will come to see me you shall be received as you ought. I should certainly pay you the first visit if my health did not compel me to keep the house; for which I offer my affectionate regrets. I shall be delighted to see my nephew, whom I invite to dine with me tomorrow—young men are less sensitive than women about the company. It will please me if Messrs. Baruch Borniche and Francois Hochon will accompany him.

Your affectionate brother,

J.-J. Rouget.

“Say we are at breakfast, but Madame Bridau will send an answer presently, and the invitations are all accepted,” said Monsieur Hochon to the servant.

The old man put a finger to his lips for silence. When the street-door closed, Monsieur Hochon, little suspecting the intimacy between his grandsons and Max, threw one of his slyest looks at his wife and Agathe, remarking, “He is just as capable of writing that note as I am of giving away twenty-five louis; it is the soldier who corresponds with us!”

“What does that portend?” asked Madame Hochon. “Well, never mind; we will answer him. As for you, monsieur,” she added to Joseph, “you must dine there; but if—”

The old lady was stopped by a look from her husband. Knowing her affection for Agathe, old Hochon feared she might leave some legacy to her goddaughter if Agathe lost the Rouget property. Though fifteen years older, the miser hoped to inherit her fortune and eventually control their property. That hope was fixed. Madame Hochon knew threatening him with her will was the best way to get concessions. Monsieur Hochon now sided with his guests. An enormous fortune was at stake; with a sense of social justice he wished it to go to natural heirs rather than pillaged by outsiders. The sooner the matter was decided, the sooner he would rid himself of the guests. The struggle between interlopers and heirs had become a fact, and Monsieur Hochon’s keen intelligence, lulled by provincial monotony, was roused. Madame Hochon was pleasantly surprised to find such an able auxiliary on the Bridau side.

Towards midday the brains of Monsieur and Madame Hochon, Agathe, and Joseph—Joseph amazed at the old people’s scrupulous choice of words—produced the following answer, concocted for Max and Flore:

My dear Brother,—If I have stayed away from Issoudun, and kept up no intercourse with any one, not even with you, the fault lies not merely with the strange and false ideas my father conceived about me, but with the joys and sorrows of my life in Paris; for if God made me a happy wife, he has also deeply afflicted me as a mother. You are aware that my son, your nephew Philippe, lies under accusation of a capital offence for his devotion to the Emperor. Therefore you can hardly be surprised if a widow, compelled to take a humble situation in a lottery-office for a living, should come to seek consolation among those among whom she was born.

The profession adopted by the son who accompanies me is one that requires great talent, many sacrifices, and prolonged studies before any results can be obtained. Glory for an artist precedes fortune; is not that to say that Joseph, though he may bring honor to the family, will still be poor? Your sister, my dear Jean-Jacques, would have borne in silence the penalties of paternal injustice, but you will pardon a mother for reminding you that you have two nephews; one who carried the Emperor’s orders at Montereau and served in the Guard at Waterloo, now in prison for his devotion; the other, from his thirteenth year, has been impelled by natural gifts to enter a difficult though glorious career.

I thank you for your letter, my dear brother, with heartfelt warmth, for my own sake and Joseph’s, who will certainly accept your invitation. Illness excuses everything, and I shall therefore go to see you in your own house. A sister is always at home with a brother, whatever life he has adopted.

I embrace you tenderly.

Agathe Rouget

“There’s the matter started. Now, when you see him,” said Monsieur Hochon to Agathe, “you must speak plainly to him about his nephews.”

Gritte returned ten minutes later to report, as is provincial custom, all she had seen and heard.

“Since yesterday Madame has had the whole house cleaned up, which she left—”

“Whom do you mean by Madame?” asked old Hochon.

“That’s what they call the Rabouilleuse,” answered Gritte. “She left the salon and Monsieur Rouget’s part of the house in a pitiable state; but since yesterday the rooms have been made like before Monsieur Maxence moved in. You can see your face on the floors. La Vedie told me Kouski rode off at five and came back at nine with provisions. It’s going to be a grand dinner—a dinner fit for the archbishop of Bourges! There’s a fine bustle; Monsieur pokes into everything. He says, ‘I want to do honor to my nephew,’ and he is delighted to find his sister so amiable. Oh! she had on such a dress! Two diamonds in her ears—Vedie says three thousand francs apiece; such lace! rings and bracelets! you’d think she was a shrine; and a silk dress like an altar cloth. She had little black satin slippers and stockings marvels—flowers in silk and openwork—and a lovely little apron Vedie says cost more than two years’ wages.”

“Well done! We shall have to dress up,” laughed the artist.

“What do you think of all this, Monsieur Hochon?” said the old lady after Gritte left.

Madame Hochon made Agathe observe her husband, who sat with his head in his hands, elbows on chair-arms, plunged in thought.

“You have to do with a Maitre Bonin!” said the old man at last. “With your ideas, young man,” he added to Joseph, “you haven’t force enough to struggle with a practised scoundrel like Maxence Gilet. No matter what I say, you will commit some folly. But tell me everything you see, hear, and do to-night. Go, and God be with you! Try to get alone with your uncle. If, despite your genius, you can’t manage it, that in itself will throw light on their scheme. But if you do get a moment alone, out of earshot, damn it, pull the wool from his eyes about the situation those two have put him in, and plead your mother’s cause.”
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
At four o’clock, Joseph crossed the avenue of weak lindens from the Rouget house to the Hochon house. Kouski announced him. The table was set in the large hall, and Joseph, who could easily distinguish his uncle, went up, kissed him, and bowed to Flore and Max.

“We have not seen each other since I came into the world, my dear uncle,” said the painter gayly; “but better late than never.”

“You are very welcome, my friend,” said the old man, looking at his nephew in a dull way.

“Madame,” Joseph said to Flore with an artist’s vivacity, “this morning I was envying my uncle the pleasure he enjoys in being able to admire you every day.”

“Isn’t she beautiful?” said the old man; his dim eyes brightened.

“Beautiful enough to be the model of a great painter.”

“Nephew,” said Rouget, whose elbow Flore was nudging, “this is Monsieur Maxence Gilet; a man who served the Emperor, like your brother, in the Imperial Guard.”

Joseph rose and bowed.

“Your brother was in the dragoons, I believe,” Maxence said. “I was only a dust-trotter.”

“On foot or on horseback,” said Flore, “you both of you risked your skins.”

Joseph and Max inspected one another. Max, dressed as a dandy, had an eager face, an air of strength, and the intellectual gray eyes inherited from his father. The painter marked him with an artist’s eye: “My uncle must be a fearful bore, and that handsome girl has compensations. It is a triangular household.”

At that instant, Baruch and François entered.

“Have you seen the tower of Issoudun?” Flore asked Joseph. “No? then if you’d like a walk before dinner, which will not be served for an hour, we’ll show you the great curiosity of the town.”

“Gladly,” said the artist, incapable of seeing any impropriety.

While Flore went to put on her bonnet, gloves, and shawl, Joseph jumped up to look at the pictures.

“Ah! you have pictures, indeed, uncle!” he cried, examining one that had struck him.

“Yes,” answered the old man. “They came to us from the Descoings, who bought them during the Revolution, when the convents and churches in Berry were dismantled.”

Joseph was lost in admiration.

“Magnificent!” he cried. “Oh! what painting! That fellow didn’t spoil his canvas. Better and better, as it is at Nicolet’s—”

“There are seven or eight very large ones up in the garret, which were kept on account of the frames,” said Gilet.

“Let me see them!” cried the artist; Max took him upstairs.

Joseph came down wildly enthusiastic. Max whispered to the Rabouilleuse, who drew the old man into a window embrasure where Joseph could hear her say: “Your nephew is a painter; you don’t care for those pictures; be kind, and give them to him.”

“It seems,” said Jean-Jacques, leaning on Flore’s arm to reach where Joseph stood before an Albano, “—it seems that you are a painter—”

“Only a ‘rapin,’” said Joseph.

“What may that be?” asked Flore.

“A beginner,” replied Joseph.

“Well,” continued Jean-Jacques, “if these pictures can be of any use to you in your business, I give them to you,—but without the frames. Oh! the frames are gilt, and besides, they are very funny; I will put—”

“Well done, uncle!” cried Joseph, enchanted; “I’ll make you copies of the same dimensions, which you can put into the frames.”

“But that will take your time, and you will want canvas and colors,” said Flore. “You will have to spend money. Come, Père Rouget, offer your nephew a hundred francs for each copy; here are twenty-seven pictures, and I think there are eleven very big ones in the garret which ought to cost double,—call the whole four thousand francs. Oh, yes,” she went on, turning to Joseph, “your uncle can well afford to pay you four thousand francs for the copies, since he keeps the frames—but bless me! you’ll want frames; and they say frames cost more than pictures; there’s more gold on them. Answer, monsieur,” she continued, shaking the old man’s arm. “Hein? it isn’t dear; your nephew will take four thousand francs for new pictures in the place of the old ones. It is,” she whispered, “a very good way to give him four thousand francs; he doesn’t look to me very flush—”

“Well, nephew, I will pay you four thousand francs for the copies—”

“No, no!” said the honest Joseph; “four thousand francs and the pictures, that’s too much; the pictures, don’t you see, are valuable—”

“Accept, simpleton!” said Flore; “he is your uncle, you know.”

“Very good, I accept,” said Joseph, bewildered by the good fortune; for he had recognized a Perugino.

The artist beamed as he went out of the house with the Rabouilleuse on his arm, which suited Maxence’s plans. Neither Flore, nor Rouget, nor Max, nor anyone in Issoudun knew the true value of the pictures, and Max thought he had secured Flore’s triumph for a song as she paraded before the astonished town, leaning on the arm of her master’s nephew, evidently on the best of terms with him. People flocked to their doors to see the crab-girl’s triumph. This sensation helped Max; when they returned at five o’clock, everyone talked of the cordial understanding between Max and Flore and the nephew. The incident of the pictures and the four thousand francs circulated. The dinner, at which Lousteau, one of the court judges, and the Mayor were present, was splendid and lasted five hours. By nine o’clock, at dessert, Joseph, seated opposite his uncle and between Flore and Max, had fraternized with the soldier and thought him the best fellow on earth. Joseph returned home at eleven somewhat tipsy. Rouget was carried to bed dead-drunk; he had soaked up the wine like sand.

“Well,” said Max when alone with Flore, “isn’t this better than making faces at them? The Bridaus are well received, they get presents, and are smothered with attentions; they will sing our praises and leave us in peace. Tomorrow you and I and Kouski will take down all those pictures and send them over to the painter, so that he shall see them when he wakes up. We will put the frames in the garret, and cover the walls with one of those varnished papers which represent scenes from Telemachus, such as I have seen at Monsieur Mouilleron’s.”

“Oh, that will be much prettier!” said Flore.

Next morning Joseph did not wake till midday. From his bed he saw the pictures, brought in while he slept, leaning against the opposite wall. While he examined them anew, recognizing each masterpiece and searching for signatures, his mother had gone to see and thank her brother, urged by old Hochon, who, having heard of the painter’s follies the night before, despaired of the Bridau cause.

“Your adversaries have the cunning of foxes,” he said to Agathe. “In all my days I never saw a man carry things with such a high hand as that soldier; they say war educates young men! Joseph has let himself be fooled. They have shut his mouth with wine, and those miserable pictures, and four thousand francs! Your artist hasn’t cost Maxence much!”

The long-headed old man instructed Madame Bridau about a line of conduct: enter into Maxence’s ideas, cajole Flore, set up intimacy, and thus obtain a few moments’ interview with Jean-Jacques alone. Madame Bridau was warmly received by her brother, to whom Flore had taught her lesson. The old man was ill from his excesses. Agathe, under the circumstances, could scarcely at once speak of family matters; Max thought it proper and magnanimous to leave brother and sister alone. The calculation was a good one. Poor Agathe found her brother so ill she would not deprive him of Madame Brazier’s care.

“Besides,” she said to the old bachelor, “I wish to know a person to whom I am grateful for the happiness of my brother.”

These words pleased the old man, who rang for Madame Flore. Flore, not far off, showed the most servile attentions and utmost tenderness to her master, fussing over him like a new bride, and the poor creature received it with feeling.

“We owe you much gratitude, mademoiselle,” said Agathe, “for the proofs of attachment you have long given my brother, and for the way in which you watch over his happiness.”

“That is true, my dear Agathe,” said the old man; “she has taught me what happiness is; she is a woman of excellent qualities.”

“And therefore, my dear brother, you ought to have recompensed Mademoiselle by making her your wife. Yes! I am too sincere in my religion not to wish to see you obey the precepts of the church. You would each be more tranquil in mind if you were not at variance with morality and the laws. I have come here, dear brother, to ask for help in my affliction; but do not suppose that we wish to make any remonstrance as to the manner in which you may dispose of your property—”

“Madame,” said Flore, “we know how unjust your father was to you. Monsieur here can tell you,” she went on, looking at Jean-Jacques, “that the only quarrels we have ever had were about you. I have always told him that he owes you part of the fortune he received from his father, and your father, my benefactor,—for he was my benefactor,” she added in a tearful voice; “I shall ever remember him! But your brother, madame, has listened to reason—”

“Yes,” said the old man, “when I make my will you shall not be forgotten.”

“Don’t talk of these things, my dear brother; you do not yet know my nature.”

After such a beginning, it is easy to imagine how the visit went on. Rouget invited his sister to dinner on the next day but one.

During these three days the Knights of Idleness captured an immense quantity of rats and mice, kept them half-famished, then let four hundred and thirty-six into a grain storage one night, including some breeding mothers. They also put a dozen pigeons into the old church roof. These animals revelled, the watchman having been enticed away by drink, so he took no care of his master’s property.

Madame Bridau believed, contrary to old Hochon, that her brother had as yet made no will; she intended to ask him his intentions about Mademoiselle Brazier as soon as she could take a walk with him alone—a hope Flore and Maxence constantly dangled and disappointed.

Meantime the Knights searched for a way to put the Parisians to flight, finding none that were not impracticable follies.

At the end of a week—half the time the Parisians were to stay—the Bridaus had made no progress. “Your lawyer does not understand the provinces,” told old Hochon to Madame Bridau. “What you have come to do can’t be done in two weeks, nor in two years; you ought never to leave your brother, but live here and try to give him some ideas of religion. You cannot countermine the fortifications of Flore and Maxence without getting a priest to sap them. That is my advice, and it is high time to set about it.”

“You certainly have very singular ideas about the clergy,” said Madame Hochon to her husband.

“Bah!” the old man exclaimed, “that’s just like you pious women.”

“God would never bless an enterprise undertaken in a sacrilegious spirit,” said Madame Bridau. “Use religion for such a purpose! Why, we should be more criminal than Flore.”

This conversation took place at breakfast with François and Baruch listening.

“Sacrilege!” exclaimed old Hochon. “If some good abbé, keen as I have known many of them to be, knew what a dilemma you are in, he would not think it sacrilege to bring your brother’s lost soul back to God, and call him to repentance for his sins, by forcing him to send away the woman who causes the scandal (with a proper provision, of course), and showing him how to set his conscience at rest by giving a few thousand francs a year to the seminary of the archbishop and leaving his property to the rightful heirs.”

The passive obedience the old miser exacted from his children and grandchildren prevented Baruch and François from showing surprise, but they exchanged glances expressing how dangerous such a scheme would be to Max’s interest.

“The fact is, madame,” said Baruch, “that if you want to secure your brother’s property, the only sure and true way will be to stay in Issoudun for the necessary length of time—”

“Mother,” said Joseph hastily, “you had better write to Desroches about all this. As for me, I ask nothing more than what my uncle has already given me.”

After recognizing the great value of his thirty-nine pictures, Joseph had carefully unnailed the canvases, fastened paper over them, gummed it at the edges, and laid them one above another in an enormous wooden box, which he sent to Desroches by the carrier’s wagon, proposing to write him a letter by post. The precious freight had been sent off the night before.

“You are satisfied with a pretty poor bargain,” said Monsieur Hochon.

“I can easily get a hundred and fifty thousand francs for those pictures,” replied Joseph.

“Painter’s nonsense!” exclaimed old Hochon.

“Mother,” said Joseph, “I am going to write to Desroches and explain to him the state of things here. If he advises you to remain, you had better do so. As for your situation, we can always find you another like it.”

“My dear Joseph,” said Madame Hochon, following him as he left the table, “I don’t know anything about your uncle’s pictures, but they ought to be good, judging by the places from which they came. If they are worth only forty thousand francs—a thousand apiece—tell no one. Though my grandsons are discreet and well-behaved, they might, without intending harm, speak of this windfall; it would be known all over Issoudun; and it is very important that our adversaries should not suspect it. You behave like a child!”

In fact, before evening many persons in Issoudun, including Max, had heard this estimate, which caused a search for all old paintings and the appearance of many execrable daubs. Max repented having driven Rouget into giving away the pictures, and his rage against the heirs, after hearing from Baruch old Hochon’s ecclesiastical scheme, increased. The influence of religion on such a feeble creature as Rouget was the one fear. The news decided Maxence to turn Rouget’s investments into money and to borrow on his landed property to buy into the Funds as soon as possible; but he considered it even more important to get rid of the Parisians at once. The genius of Mascarilles and Scapins together would hardly have solved that easily.

Flore, by Max’s advice, pretended that Monsieur was too feeble to take walks and ought to have a carriage. This pretext avoided inquiry when they went to Bourges, Vierzon, Châteauroux, Vatan, and other places where the project of withdrawing investments obliged Max and Flore to be with Rouget. At the close of the week, all Issoudun was amazed to learn the old man had gone to Bourges to buy a carriage—a step the Knights regarded as favorable to the Rabouilleuse. Flore and Max selected a hideous “berlingot,” twenty-two years old, sold on the colonel’s decease, a sort of demi-fortune carriage. It only cost four hundred and fifty francs; Max bought a stout mare trained to harness and repainted the carriage dark brown, buying a tolerable harness at a bargain. The town awaited Pere Rouget’s equipage; at its first appearance every household was on its doorstep.

The second time the old bachelor went out he drove to Bourges, went before a notary, and signed a power of attorney in favor of Maxence Gilet to make all the transfers enumerated. Flore reserved to herself the business of making Monsieur sell investments in Issoudun and its neighborhood. The principal notary in Bourges was requested by Rouget to get him a loan of one hundred and forty thousand francs on his landed estate. Nothing was known at Issoudun of these secret proceedings. Maxence, a good rider, went with his horse to Bourges and back between five in the morning and five in the afternoon. Flore never left the old bachelor. Rouget consented without objection to the actions Flore dictated; but he insisted that the investment in the Funds producing fifty thousand francs a year should stand in Flore’s name as holding a life-interest only, and in his as owner of the principal. His tenacity in this domestic dispute worried Max; he thought it the result of reflections inspired by seeing the natural heirs.

Amid these movements, which Max concealed, he forgot the Spaniard and his granary. Fario came back to Issoudun to deliver his corn after various trips and manoeuvres to raise the price of cereals. The morning after his arrival he noticed the church roof black with pigeons. He hurried to the storehouse, where he found half his grain devoured. Thousands of mice and rat marks showed further ruin. The church was a Noah’s ark. But anger turned the Spaniard white when he found the grain at the bottom sprouting from water introduced by tin tubes into the pile of wheat. The pigeons and rats could be instinct, but the wetting showed human hand.

Fario sat on the chapel steps with his head between his hands. After half an hour he spied the squirrel Goddet had sent him playing on a cross-beam. He rose, turned to his watchman with a calm face, made no complaint, but hired laborers to gather sound grain and spread the moist to dry, saving as much as possible; after estimating his losses at about three fifths, he attended to filling his orders. But his previous manipulations had raised the price of cereals, and he lost on the three fifths he had to buy to fill orders; his losses were really over half. The Spaniard, with no enemies, at once attributed this revenge to Gilet. He was convinced Maxence and others were the authors of the nocturnal mischief and intended to ruin him. It was a matter of over three thousand francs—nearly his whole capital since the peace. Driven by the desire for vengeance, the man displayed the persistence of a detective. Hiding at night in different parts of Issoudun, he soon acquired proof of the Knights’ proceedings: he saw them, counted them, watched their rendezvous, and knew of their suppers at Mère Cognette’s; he lay in wait, witnessed their deeds, and thus became well informed of their nocturnal habits.

In spite of Max’s journeys and preoccupations, he did not neglect his nightly employments—first, because he did not wish his comrades to suspect his operations with Pere Rouget’s property; second, to keep the Knights in hand. They were convened for a prank to be talked of for years: poisoned meat was to be thrown to every watchdog in town and environs. Fario overheard them congratulating each other after a supper on the probable success of this massacre and laughing over the general mourning it would cause.

“It will make people forget Fario’s cart,” said Goddet.

Fario needed no further proof; his mind was made up.

After three weeks in Issoudun, Agathe and Madame Hochon were convinced of the old miser’s observation that it would take years to destroy Max and Flore’s influence. She had made no progress in Jean-Jacques’s confidence and was never left alone with him. On the other hand, Mademoiselle Brazier triumphed openly by taking Agathe to drive in the caleche, sitting beside her on the back seat while Monsieur Rouget and his nephew occupied the front. Mother and son impatiently awaited an answer to the confidential letter they had written to Desroches. The day before the night on which the dogs were to be poisoned, Joseph, nearly bored to death in Issoudun, received two letters: one from the great painter Schinner and the second from Desroches.

Schinner’s letter, from Beaumont-sur-Oise, said in effect that Joseph was recommended for the chateau de Presles for the Comte de Serizy; that Schinner was off to Italy; and that Joseph should take his place at the Salon if need be. Desroches’s letter gave practical advice:

My dear Joseph,—Your Monsieur Hochon strikes me as an old man full of common-sense, and you give me a high idea of his methods; he is perfectly right. My advice, since you ask it, is that your mother should remain at Issoudun with Madame Hochon, paying a small board—say four hundred francs a year—to reimburse her hosts. Madame Bridau ought, in my opinion, to follow Monsieur Hochon’s advice in everything; for your excellent mother will have many scruples in dealing with persons who have no scruple at all, and whose behavior to her is a masterstroke of policy. That Maxence, you are right enough, is dangerous. He is another Philippe, but of a different calibre. The scoundrel makes his vices serve his fortunes, and gets his amusement gratis; whereas your brother’s follies are never useful to him. All that you say alarms me, but I could do no good by going to Issoudun. Monsieur Hochon, acting behind your mother, will be more useful to you than I. As for you, you had better come back here; you are good for nothing in a matter which requires continual attention, careful observation, servile civilities, discretion in speech, and a dissimulation of manner and gesture wholly against the grain of artists.

If they have told you no will has been made, you may be sure they have one. But wills can be revoked, and as long as your uncle lives he is susceptible of being worked upon by remorse and religion. Your inheritance will be the result of a combat between the Church and the Rabouilleuse. There will come a time when that woman will lose her grip and religion will be all-powerful. So long as your uncle makes no gift of the property during his lifetime, and does not change his estate, all may come right when religion gets the upper hand. For this reason you must beg Monsieur Hochon to keep an eye on the condition of your uncle’s property. It is necessary to know if the real estate is mortgaged, and if so, where and in whose name the proceeds are invested. It is so easy to terrify an old man with fears about his life, in case you find him despoiling his own property, that almost any heir with a little adroitness could stop the spoliation at its outset. But how should your mother, with ignorance of the world and religious ideas, manage such an affair? I cannot throw more light. All you have done probably gave the alarm, and your adversaries may already have secured themselves—

“That is what I call an opinion in good shape,” exclaimed Monsieur Hochon, proud of being appreciated by a Parisian lawyer.

“Oh! Desroches is a famous fellow,” answered Joseph.

“It would be well to read that letter to the two women,” said the old man.

“There it is,” said Joseph, giving it to him; “as to me, I want to be off to-morrow; and I am now going to say good-by to my uncle.”

“Ah!” said Monsieur Hochon, “I see that Monsieur Desroches tells you in a postscript to burn the letter.”

“You can burn it after showing it to my mother,” said the painter.

Joseph dressed, crossed the little square, and called on his uncle, who was finishing breakfast. Max and Flore were at table.

“Don’t disturb yourself, my dear uncle; I have only come to say good-by.”

“You are going?” said Max, exchanging glances with Flore.

“Yes; I have work at the chateau of Monsieur de Serizy, and I am all the more glad because his arm is long enough to do a service to my poor brother in the Chamber of Peers.”

“Well, well, go and work,” said old Rouget, with a silly air. Joseph thought him extraordinarily changed within a few days. “Men must work—I am sorry you are going.”

“Oh! my mother will be here some time longer,” remarked Joseph.

Max made a movement with his lips which Flore observed, meaning: “They are going to try the plan Baruch warned me of.”

“I am very glad I came,” said Joseph, “for I have had the pleasure of making your acquaintance and you have enriched my studio—”

“Yes,” said Flore, “instead of enlightening your uncle on the value of his pictures, which is now estimated at over one hundred thousand francs, you have packed them off in a hurry to Paris. Poor dear man! he is no better than a baby! We have just been told of a little treasure at Bourges,—what did they call it? a Poussin,—which was in the choir of the cathedral before the Revolution and is now worth, all by itself, thirty thousand francs.”

“That was not right of you, my nephew,” said Jean-Jacques, at a sign from Max, which Joseph could not see.

“Come now, frankly,” said the soldier, laughing, “on your honor, what should you say those pictures were worth? You’ve made an easy haul out of your uncle! and right enough, too—uncles are made to be pillaged. Nature deprived me of uncles, but damn it, if I’d had any I should have shown them no mercy.”

“Did you know, monsieur,” said Flore to Rouget, “what your pictures were worth? How much did you say, Monsieur Joseph?”

“Well,” answered the painter, red as a beetroot,—“the pictures are certainly worth something.”

“They say you estimated them to Monsieur Hochon at one hundred and fifty thousand francs,” said Flore; “is that true?”

“Yes,” said the painter, with childlike honesty.

“And did you intend,” said Flore to the old man, “to give a hundred and fifty thousand francs to your nephew?”

“Never, never!” cried Jean-Jacques, on whom Flore had fixed her eye.

“There is one way to settle all this,” said the painter, “and that is to return them to you, uncle.”

“No, no, keep them,” said the old man.

“I shall send them back to you,” said Joseph, wounded by Max and Flore’s offensive silence. “There is something in my brushes which will make my fortune, without owing anything to any one, even an uncle. My respects to you, mademoiselle; good-day, monsieur—”

Joseph crossed the square in a state of irritation artists can imagine. The entire Hochon family were in the salon. Seeing Joseph gesticulate and talking to himself, they asked him what was the matter. The painter, as open as the day, related before Baruch and François the scene; two hours later it was the town’s talk, embroidered with various circumstances. Some persons insisted the painter was maltreated by Max; others that he had misbehaved to Flore and been turned out.

“What a child your son is!” said Hochon to Madame Bridau; “the booby is the dupe of a scene they have been keeping for the last day of his visit. Max and the Rabouilleuse have known the value of those pictures for the last two weeks—ever since he foolishly told it before my grandsons, who blurted it out to all the world. Your artist had better have taken himself off without taking leave.”

“My son has done right to return the pictures if they are really so valuable,” said Agathe.

“If they are worth, as he says, two hundred thousand francs,” said old Hochon, “it was folly to put himself in a way to be obliged to return them. You might have had that, at least, out of the property; whereas, as things are going now, you won’t get anything. And this scene with Joseph is almost a reason why your brother should refuse to see you again.”
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Between midnight and one, the Knights of Idleness began their nightly distribution of food to the dogs of the town, an expedition that lasted until three, when they supped at Cognette’s. At half-past four they crept home. As Max turned from the rue l’Avenier into the Grande rue, Fario, lying in wait in a recess, struck at his heart, withdrew the blade, and fled toward Vilatte, wiping the knife on his handkerchief. The Spaniard washed the handkerchief in the Riviere forcee and returned quietly to his lodgings at Saint-Paterne, entering by a window he had left open and going to bed; later a watchman found him asleep.

Max uttered a fearful cry as he fell. Lousteau-Prangin, a judge’s son and distant relation of the sub-delegate, and young Goddet, who lived at the lower end of the Grande rue, ran up, shouting, “They are killing Max! Help!” The town, used to such false alarms, remained asleep. When they reached him, Max had fainted and they roused Monsieur Goddet, the surgeon. Max had recognized Fario; but when he revived among bystanders and felt his wound was not mortal, he seized the chance to make capital of the attack and whispered, “I think I recognized that cursed painter!”

Lousteau-Prangin ran to his father the judge. Max was carried home by Cognette, young Goddet, and two others, while Mere Cognette and Monsieur Goddet walked beside the stretcher. Those who carried him glanced at Monsieur Hochon’s door and saw Hochon’s servant sweeping the steps. Gritte, like most provincial households, was early astir. The few words Max had spoken roused Monsieur Goddet’s suspicions. He asked the woman if Monsieur Joseph Bridau was in bed.

“He went out at half-past four,” she answered. “He walked up and down his room all night.”

Such an answer produced exclamations of horror; Gritte, curious, came over and saw Max in his bloody shirt half-fainting on a mattress. Artists can guess what had kept Joseph restless all night: he had dreaded the gossip at Issoudun, imagined the town would say he had fleeced his uncle and been deceitful. He had early at dawn gone to the poplar avenue to walk and calm himself.

While Joseph comforted himself by vowing never to return to Issoudun, Max prepared a theatrical outrage for his sensitive spirit. Monsieur Goddet, after probing the wound and finding that a small pocket-book had deflected the knife so the blow was serious but not mortal, did what country surgeons do to enhance their credit: he “did not answer for the patient’s life,” dressed him, delivered a grave verdict to the Rabouilleuse, Jean-Jacques Rouget, Kouski, and the Vedie, then left the house. The Rabouilleuse wept over Max; Kouski and the Vedie declared the captain was likely to die. Nearly two hundred people gathered about the place Saint-Jean and the two Narettes.

“I sha’n’t be a month in bed; and I know who struck the blow,” Max whispered to Flore. “We’ll profit by it to get rid of the Parisians. I said I thought I recognized the painter; pretend I’m expected to die and try to have Joseph Bridau arrested. Let him taste prison for a couple of days and his mother will be off to Paris when she gets him out. Then we needn’t fear the priests they talk of setting on the old fool.”

When Flore came downstairs the crowd was ready to take the impression she meant to give. She went out in tears and, sobbing, related how the painter, “who had just the face for that sort of thing,” had been angry with Max the night before about some pictures he had “wormed out” of Pere Rouget.

“That brigand—look at him!—thinks that if Max were dead his uncle would leave him his fortune,” she cried. “The old one told me Max is Doctor Rouget’s son!”

“Ah! he meant to do the deed before he left Issoudun; he chose his time, for he was going away today,” said one of the Knights of Idleness.

“Max hasn’t an enemy in Issoudun,” said another.

“Besides, Max recognized the painter,” said the Rabouilleuse.

“Where’s that cursed Parisian? Let us find him!” they cried.

“Find him? Why, he left Monsieur Hochon’s at daybreak.”

A Knight of Idleness ran to Monsieur Mouilleron. The crowd grew, the tumult threatened. Groups filled the Grande-Narette and formed before Saint-Jean; a mass assembled at porte Vilatte. Lousteau-Prangin, Monsieur Mouilleron the commissary of police, the lieutenant of gendarmes, and two men struggled through hedges of people whose cries prejudiced them against the Parisian, though he was unjustly accused—circumstances, however, pointed against him.

After conferring with Max, Monsieur Mouilleron sent the commissary and a sergeant with one gendarme to inspect the “theatre of the crime.” Messieurs Mouilleron and Lousteau-Prangin, with the gendarme lieutenant, crossed to the Hochon house now guarded by two gendarmes in the garden and two at the door. The crowd swelled; the whole town surged in the Grande rue.

Gritte rushed, terrified, to inform Monsieur Hochon: “Monsieur, we shall be pillaged! The town is in revolt; Monsieur Maxence Gilet has been assassinated; they say Monsieur Joseph did it!”

Monsieur Hochon, alarmed for his gold, dressed quickly, peered out, then bolted the house. He learned from Gritte that his guest had left at daybreak—after pacing his room all night—and not yet returned. Madame Hochon, already awake from the noise, heard the frightful tidings.

“He is innocent, of course,” she said.

“Before his innocence can be proved, the crowd may pillage us,” replied Hochon, white with fear.

“Where is Agathe?”

“Sound asleep.”

“So much the better,” said Madame Hochon. “A shock might kill the poor child.”

But Agathe woke half-dressed, disturbed by evasive answers and the turmoil. She found Madame Hochon pale, weeping at a salon window beside her husband.

“Courage, my child. God sends us our afflictions,” the old lady said. “Joseph is accused—”

“Of what?”

“Of a bad action he could never have committed,” Madame Hochon answered.

Seeing the lieutenant of gendarmes enter with the magistrates, Agathe fainted.

“There now!” said Monsieur Hochon. “Carry off Madame Bridau; women are only in the way at these times. Take her to her room and stay there, both of you. Sit down, gentlemen. The mistake to which we owe your visit will soon, I hope, be cleared up.”

“Even if it should be a mistake,” said Monsieur Mouilleron, “the crowd is so exasperated I fear for the accused’s safety. I should like to arrest him; that might pacify these people.”

“Who would have believed Monsieur Maxence Gilet inspired so much affection?” asked Lousteau-Prangin.

“One of my men says twelve hundred more are coming from the faubourg de Rome,” said the lieutenant; “they threaten to kill the assassin.”

“Where is your guest?” asked Mouilleron of Hochon.

“He went to walk in the country, I believe.”

“Call Gritte,” said the judge gravely. “The crime was committed not far from here.”

The three functionaries exchanged looks.

“I never liked that painter’s face,” said the lieutenant.

When Gritte appeared trembling, Lousteau-Prangin asked if she saw Monsieur Joseph leave that morning. She confirmed it: “Just as I was getting up; he walked about all night, and was dressed when I came downstairs.” Lousteau ordered warrants fetched. Monsieur Hochon pleaded caution: Joseph may have been agitated for other reasons—he meant to return to Paris that day for business in which Gilet and Mademoiselle Brazier had questioned his honor. Mouilleron added that the quarrel over the pictures yesterday had been hot; with artists it can be a word and a blow. Lousteau declared no one in Issoudun had cause to kill Gilet. Hochon noted, “But what was Monsieur Gilet doing in the streets at four in the morning?”

A clamour rose from the other end of town, like thunder: “Here he is! Here he is—he’s arrested!” Joseph, returning quietly past the mill at Landrole to breakfast, had been seen at the place Misere. Two gendarmes ran in time to snatch him from the faubourg de Rome inhabitants, who had already pinioned him and threatened him with death.

“Give way! Give way!” cried the gendarmes as they formed a protective escort.

“You see, monsieur,” one who held the painter said, “it concerns our skin as well as yours. Innocent or guilty, we must protect you. The people adore Monsieur Gilet and want to take justice into their own hands. Didn’t we see them in 1830 dusting the jackets of the tax-gatherers?”

Joseph, pale, forced himself to go on. Amid hoots, insults, and stones, he was led from place Misere to place Saint-Jean; the gendarmes drew sabres, one was wounded, and Joseph received missiles on his legs, shoulders, and hat.

“We must disperse the crowd; I see but one way,” said the lieutenant to the magistrates. “Take Monsieur Bridau to the Palais accompanied by all of you; my gendarmes will make a circle. One can’t answer for anything before a furious crowd of six thousand.”

“You are right,” said Monsieur Hochon, trembling for his gold.

“Do you wish your friend’s house pillaged?” asked the lieutenant. “Could we beat back a crowd pushed from behind by an angry populace that knows nothing of legal forms?”

“That will do; let us go; we can explain later,” said Joseph, who had recovered composure.

“Give way, friends! He is arrested, and we are taking him to the Palais,” the lieutenant cried. “Respect the law!” said Mouilleron.

“Wouldn’t you prefer to see him guillotined?” jeered a gendarme at a group.

“Yes, yes, they shall guillotine him!” one madman shouted; the women echoed, “They are going to guillotine him!” By the time they reached the end of the Grande-Narette the crowd cried: “They are taking him to the guillotine! They found the knife upon him! That’s what Parisians are! He carries crime on his face!”

Though blood rushed to his head, Joseph walked from place Saint-Jean to the Palais with calm. In Monsieur Lousteau-Prangin’s private office he declared his innocence: he had been out past five, stood at half-past five before Saint-Cyr’s facade, talked with the sexton, crossed the vegetable market where women had assembled, passed place Misere, went as far as the mill of Landrole by the Pont aux Anes and watched the ducks five or six minutes—miller’s men must have seen him—and then followed the avenue to Tivoli where he spoke with the gardener. He urged the magistrates to verify these facts and begged not to be arrested, promising to remain in the office until cleared.

These sensible, confident statements impressed the magistrates.

“Yes, summon all those persons,” said Mouilleron; “but it is more than a day’s affair. Make up your mind to be imprisoned in the Palais.”

“Provided I can write to my mother to reassure her—oh, you can read the letter,” Joseph added.

This just request was granted, and Joseph wrote to his mother assuring her the mistake could be rectified and that he expected to be free by evening or tomorrow.

Madame Bridau, suffering a nervous attack and medicated by Monsieur Goddet without relief, read the letter and was soothed into the usual depressed state. When Goddet returned, he found her regretting leaving Paris.

“How is Monsieur Gilet?” asked Madame Hochon of Goddet.

“His wound, though serious, is not mortal,” the doctor replied. “With a month’s nursing he will be all right. I left him writing to Monsieur Mouilleron to request Joseph’s release,” he added. “Oh! Max is a fine fellow. He then remembered something proving the assassin was not your son; the man wore list shoes, whereas it is certain Monsieur Joseph left in boots—”

“Ah! God forgive him the harm he has done me—”

A man had left Max, after dark, a typed note that read: “Captain Gilet ought not to let an innocent man suffer. He who struck the blow promises not to strike again if Monsieur Gilet will have Monsieur Joseph Bridau set at liberty,” without naming the attacker. After reading and burning this letter, Max wrote to Monsieur Mouilleron relating the list-shoe detail from Goddet and begged Joseph’s release and asked Mouilleron to visit so he might explain more.

By the time Max’s letter arrived, Lousteau-Prangin had verified Joseph’s account through the bell-ringer, market-women, washerwomen, and the miller’s men. Max’s note clinched Joseph’s innocence, and Monsieur Mouilleron himself escorted him back to the Hochons’. His mother greeted him with overflowing tenderness; the poor misunderstood son was grateful: he had found affection from misfortune.

“Oh,” said Monsieur Mouilleron with self-satisfaction, “I knew by your look before the crowd that you were innocent; but the only way to protect you was to arrest you as we did. You carried your head high.”

“I was thinking of something else,” replied Joseph. “An officer once told me he had been stopped in Dalmatia under similar circumstances returning from a walk. The recollection made me think of painting a revolt of 1793. Besides, I kept saying, Blackguard that I am! I deserve this for coming to look after an inheritance instead of painting in my studio.”

“If you allow me a word of advice,” said the procureur du roi, “take the postmaster’s carriage to-night and return to Paris by the diligence from Bourges.”

“That is my advice too,” said Monsieur Hochon, burning to see them go.

“My most earnest wish is to get away,” said Agathe, kissing Madame Hochon’s hand. “When shall I see you again?”

“Ah, my dear, never until we meet above. We have suffered enough here below,” the old woman answered in a low voice.

Shortly after, Monsieur Rouget surprised them with a farewell visit; Jean-Jacques came to bid his sister good-by and offer his caleche for the drive to Bourges. “Ah! your pictures have been a great evil to us,” said Agathe. “Keep them, my sister,” said Rouget, who still doubted their value. He let them kiss him and went away without manifesting feeling. Baruch, at a hint from his grandfather, had seen the postmaster. At eleven that night the two Parisians, ensconced in a wicker cabriolet drawn by one horse and a postilion, left Issoudun. Adolphine and Madame Hochon parted with tears; they alone regretted Joseph and Agathe.

“They are gone!” said Francois Hochon, going with the Rabouilleuse into Max’s bedroom.

“Well done! The trick succeeded,” answered Max, now tired and feverish.

“What did you say to old Mouilleron?” asked Francois.

“I told him I had given my assassin some cause to waylay me; that he was dangerous and likely, if I pursued the affair, to kill me before capture. Consequently, I begged Mouilleron and Prangin to make an ostensible active search but really to let the assassin go in peace, unless they wished to see me dead.”

“I do hope, Max,” said Flore, “that you will be quiet at night for some time to come.”

“At any rate, we are delivered from the Parisians!” cried Max. “The fellow who stabbed me had no idea what a service he was doing us.”

The next day the Parisians’ departure was celebrated as a provincial victory over Paris by nearly everyone in Issoudun, except the sober, staid inhabitants who shared Monsieur and Madame Hochon’s view. A few of Max’s friends spoke harshly of the Bridaus.

“Do those Parisians think we are all idiots?” cried one. “They came to fleece and have been shorn themselves,” said another. “They actually consulted a lawyer in Paris—what plan had they?” “A plan to get possession of old Rouget. But the Parisians were not clever enough; that lawyer can’t crow over us Berrichons!” “How abominable!” “That’s Paris for you!” “The Rabouilleuse knew they came to attack her, and she defended herself.” “She did gloriously right!”

To the townspeople the Bridaus were Parisians and foreigners; they preferred Max and Flore.

We can imagine the comfort with which Joseph and Agathe re-entered their lodging in the rue Mazarin. On the journey Joseph recovered spirits lost by arrest and a day’s confinement; but his mother could not be cheered. The Court of Peers was about to try the military conspirators, and that kept Agathe anxious. Philippe’s conduct, despite a clever defender recommended by Desroches, raised suspicions unfavorable to his character. To escape hearing about the trial—lasting twenty days—Joseph, after briefing Desroches fully on Issoudun, set off with Mistigris for the Comte de Serizy’s chateau.

Contemporary histories need not be repeated here: whether Philippe had played an agreed part or was truly an informer, he was condemned to five years’ surveillance by the police and ordered to leave Paris that day for Autun, the director-general’s choice for his exile. The punishment resembled parole with a town for a prison. Learning that the Comte de Serizy, one of the peers on the court-martial, was employing Joseph to decorate his chateau at Presles, Desroches sought an audience and found Monsieur de Serizy favorably disposed; they had met. Desroches explained the brothers’ financial plight, recalled their father’s services and neglect under the Restoration, and urged the minister to induce the director-general of police to change Philippe’s residence from Autun to Issoudun and to grant a dole of sixty francs a month to the former lieutenant-colonel, a matter of shame for the minister of war to deny.

“I will obtain all you ask of me, for I think it just,” replied the count.

Three days later Desroches, armed with authority, fetched Philippe from the Court of Peers’ prison and took him to his house in the rue de Bethizy. There the young barrister read Philippe a plain, unanswerable lecture in which lawyers reduce clients’ sentiment to simple, blunt terms. He humbled the former staff-officer by reproaching his reckless dissipations, his mother’s misfortunes, and the death of Madame Descoings, then explained Issoudun’s situation and probed Maxence Gilet and the Rabouilleuse’s characters. Philippe, who grasped such matters keenly, listened more to this part than to the reproaches.

“Under these circumstances,” Desroches continued, “you can repair some of the injury you have done—so far as possible; you cannot restore life to the poor mother you have nearly killed. But you alone can—”

“What can I do?” asked Philippe.

“I have obtained a change of residence for you from Autun to Issoudun.”

Philippe’s sunken, almost sinister face brightened.

“And,” Desroches went on, “you alone can recover your uncle’s inheritance; half may already be in Gilet’s hands. I suggest no plan; everything depends on local circumstances. You have to deal with a strong, astute man. The way he tried to recover the pictures he’d given to Joseph, and the audacity with which he laid the crime on your brother, shows he’s capable of anything. Be prudent; behave properly out of policy if not decency. Without telling Joseph, whose pride as an artist would revolt, I have sent the pictures to Monsieur Hochon, telling him to give them up to no one but you. By the way, Maxence Gilet is a brave man.”

“So much the better,” said Philippe; “I count on his courage for success; a coward would leave Issoudun.”

“Consult Monsieur Hochon,” urged Desroches. “You have your orders from the police; I have taken your place in the Orleans diligence for half-past seven tonight. I suppose your trunk is ready; come and dine.”

“I own nothing but what I have on my back,” said Philippe, opening his threadbare blue overcoat; “but I only need three things, which you must tell Giroudeau, the uncle of Finot, to send me—my sabre, my sword, and my pistols.”

“You need more than that,” said the lawyer, shuddering at his client’s appearance. “You will receive a quarterly stipend which will clothe you decently.”

“Bless me! are you here, Godeschal?” cried Philippe, recognizing in Desroches’s head-clerk the brother of Mariette.

“Yes, I have been with Monsieur Desroches for two months.”

“And he will stay with me till he gets business of his own,” said Desroches.

“How is Mariette?” asked Philippe, moved.

“She is getting ready for the opening of the new theatre.”

“It would cost her little trouble to get my sentence remitted,” said Philippe. “However, as she chooses!”

After a meagre dinner—Desroches boarded his head-clerk—the two lawyers put the political convict in the diligence and wished him good luck.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
On the second of November, All-Souls’ day, Philippe Bridau presented himself to the commissary of police at Issoudun to have his arrival noted; following the official’s advice he took rooms in the rue l’Avenier. News that an officer banished for a recent military conspiracy had come to town spread quickly, and created more stir when people learned he was brother to the painter who had been wrongly accused. Maxence Gilet, now recovered from his wound, had succeeded in converting all Pere Rouget’s mortgages into cash and placing the proceeds in the “grand-livre.” The loan of 140,000 francs on Rouget’s real estate caused a scandal—everything leaks fast in the provinces. Monsieur Hochon, anxious on the Bridaus’ behalf and still smarting from the terror he had felt at the thought of his house being ransacked, questioned old Monsieur Heron, the Bourges notary, about it.

“If not for me,” Heron declared, “old Rouget would have kept the 50,000 francs’ income in Maxence Gilet’s name. I advised Mademoiselle Brazier to rely on the will, and cautioned Maxence and his mistress to let this sudden change be forgotten.” Hochon urged the protection of the Bridaus.

Maxence and Flore, secure against attack, were gleeful at another nephew’s arrival; they knew they could have old Rouget sign a power of attorney transferring funds to Max or Flore if danger threatened. If the will leaving Flore the principal were revoked, the 50,000-franc income and the newly extracted mortgage of 140,000 would be good consolation.

The day after arriving, Philippe called on his uncle around ten, in shabby clothes. Flore shivered at his repulsive appearance; Gilet felt the same preternatural chill that warns of a hidden enemy. Philippe’s ruined blue greatcoat was buttoned tight to the throat; his trousers were ragged at the hems, his boots leaked mud, his hat was greasy, his malacca cane dull. His face showed the exhaustion of a gambler: sallow, leathery skin, sunken cheeks, red-rimmed lids, a menacing brow. Bald except for a fringe of hair, his brilliant eyes had a cold steel-blue. He introduced himself hoarsely as Philippe Bridau, a lieutenant-colonel ill-treated by the Bourbons, and complained that he had to live on a full pay of sixty francs a month by police order. He made a pointed show of disdain for relying on his uncle.

“Good-morning, uncle,” he said, offering his hand; Rouget took it. He declared he would sooner cut off his hand than beg from his uncle after the way his mother and brother had been treated in Issoudun, but he had felt it proper to pay respects. Flore invited him to breakfast; he declined, saying he had already eaten. He hinted that he must safeguard the Bridaus’ honor, spoke curtly about pictures at Monsieur Hochon’s for identification, and left. His tone and the violence with which he slammed the door shocked Flore and Max more than his looks had.

Max and Flore watched him cross to the Hochons’. Gilet, though furious, kept his temper; he knew the need for political prudence. Flore urged Rouget not to admit him again.

Later Philippe entered the Hochon house where curiosity was thick. Madame Hochon shuddered at the father of Agathe’s griefs and the murderer of Madame Descoings; others felt unease. Philippe politely explained he had come to introduce himself and manage living on sixty francs. He spoke well, admitted past faults, and promised reformation. He praised Lousteau the journalist as a “rara avis,” which won the old lady’s favor. Madame Hochon advised him gently; he said prison had taught him much and he would live another life.

Outside, he took Monsieur Hochon aside. In a whisper he told the miser the town’s gossip could be ruinous and they must avoid being suspected of advising him publicly. Desroches had instructed Philippe to use Hochon’s counsel discreetly; therefore they agreed to meet “accidentally” in open places when needed. Philippe asked for names of veterans in Issoudun who were not Maxence’s friends—he wanted allies. Hochon named Mignonnet, an artillery captain of the Guard, and Captain Carpentier, a cavalry officer who had replaced Gilet as mayoral clerk and befriended Mignonnet. They discussed Philippe’s need for work; a mutual insurance agency might pay fifty francs a month—enough.

Within a week Philippe had bought on credit a decent suit, boots, gloves, and a hat. Giroudeau sent linen and weapons and a letter to Carpentier, who welcomed him and introduced him to Mignonnet, recommending him as a man of merit. Philippe narrated parts of the conspiracy to them—in such a way as to win their admiration. He explained the recent conspiracy was a continuation of earlier efforts by the old army against the Bourbons: a vast northern operation and a Paris diversion. Betrayed, the leaders had reduced the Paris plot to a barrack affair, and when the bigger scheme failed Philippe was to break links and let only the smaller plot be exposed; his apparent misery and ruined costume served to minimize the conspiracy’s size in the authorities’ eyes. He had posed as a man playing both sides, preserving favor with influential figures while waiting to join whichever party served his interests best. Carpentier and Mignonnet, impressed by his explanation and apparent self-devotion, thought him a man of statecraft and brought him into their circle.

They recommended him for a post at the insurance office Hochon had mentioned, requiring only three hours of his day. Philippe frequented their club; his manners and the officers’ praise earned him respect. Determined to seem sober and respectable, and to lull Gilet’s suspicions, he pretended simple-mindedness while quietly observing his uncle’s property and plotting. Hochon had given him details of Rouget’s wealth, which stirred his covetousness. He and Hochon agreed not to alarm Maxence; if Rouget were spirited off to Bourges the game would be lost.

Philippe dined once a week with Mignonnet, once with Carpentier, and every Thursday with Hochon; soon he had engagements most nights. He never spoke of his uncle or his brother and mother except in connection with their stay in Issoudun. The three officers, decorated men in town, walked together daily; Philippe had the advantage of a rosette and carried himself like a man of consequence. To Max’s friends Philippe’s demeanor and story made him seem a “sabreur,” a brave front of the old army.

“His conduct before the Court of Peers shows him either a dupe or a spy,” said Max; others defended him. Philippe, wishing to conceal details of his situation, lived in a secluded house in the faubourg Saint-Paterne with a garden; here he fenced with Carpentier, reviving his skills and learning new tricks. He practiced pistols with Mignonnet and Carpentier, ostensibly for sport but really to make Max believe he preferred pistols in a duel. When he met Gilet he waited for a bow and returned it with a cavalier touch to the hat, as an officer to a private. Gilet gave no sign of impatience and never spoke of Bridau at the Cognettes’, though the pranks of the Order of Idleness were temporarily suspended since Fario’s attack.

Philippe’s contempt for Gilet and Gilet’s restrained bearing became the talk of Issoudun. Some thought Philippe was setting a trap. Potel suggested Max might be baiting him; others speculated about what might grow from the enmity. Rumor set the two up as adversaries.

Philippe had investigated the circumstances of his brother’s arrest and Gilet’s and the Rabouilleuse’s past, and he was brought into contact with Fario the Spaniard. Finding Fario reliable, Philippe made him a confidant and promised him indemnity for past losses—Fario, a willing henchman, put his knowledge of the Knights of Idleness and his services at Philippe’s disposal. The town, abuzz with gossip, expected a deadly clash between the two men who intensely despised one another.

Toward the end of November, Philippe told Hochon he had discovered that Hochon’s grandsons Baruch and Francois were intimate with Maxence and that they had spied in his house, informing Max of what was said there. He claimed to have overheard them and learned from Fario of their nocturnal visits to drinking-shops in Maxence’s company; they owed Maxence over three thousand francs. Philippe suspected Max and the Rabouilleuse intended to secure the 50,000-franc income and then run away and marry. They must learn what was happening under Rouget’s roof, Philippe said, but he did not know how to proceed. Hochon promised to think.

Jean-Jacques Rouget suffered as never before since Philippe’s first visit. Flore, fearing some evil to Max, planned to leave Issoudun and marry Max in Paris after getting the Funds’ income transferred. Rouget refused to transfer, insisting Flore be sole heir—he believed she would abandon him if made rich. She tried cajolery and then coldness; Vedie found Rouget one morning with swollen eyes from weeping. For a week he breakfasted alone, perhaps on meager fare.

The day after Philippe’s talk with Hochon, he went to Rouget’s house again. Flore played tender and solicitous in his presence; Gilet stayed away. Philippe, discerning danger, judged it time to strike. He forced Flore to look him in the face and demanded to know if Rouget was free to walk alone with him. Flore stammered that he was. Philippe insisted Rouget accompany him on foot, implying that if he didn’t, the town’s claim that Rouget was under Flore’s dominion would be confirmed. Seeing Rouget hesitate, Philippe pretended to go; he caught Flore making a menacing gesture at her uncle, and told Rouget to meet him at the door in ten minutes or he would take other measures. He left for the Hochons’.

At nine that night, Hochon, Madame Hochon, Monsieur Heron the notary, and the grandchildren gathered. Hochon, enraged, denounced Baruch and Francois for their nocturnal life with Maxence and for having informed the Knights of Idleness; he accused them of betraying secrets for money and even of causing past crimes by transmitting plans. He read the guardianship accounts: the Borniche children’s net fortune was 70,000 francs, a part of their mother’s dowry, but Hochon, as creditor to his daughter, controlled much of it. Baruch’s portion was twenty thousand francs. Hochon told them he would release their property to them but no longer tolerate their conduct; he discharged his guardianship and made their futures contingent on strict behavior. Baruch and Francois were stunned.

After Heron read the accounts, Hochon told Baruch he would be sent to Paris to study banking under Mongenod; Francois, who was in debt, would have only six hundred francs a year and would go to Poitiers to study law. Hochon gloated over his quick vengeance: he would watch them and test them by years of conduct. Having settled the legal business and obtained receipts, Heron was preparing to leave when Philippe arrived. Madame Hochon withdrew with her grandsons to see what effect the scene had produced.

Philippe and Hochon spoke in the window embrasure. Hochon had consulted Heron and learned that the security for the 50,000-franc income could not be sold without the owner’s signature or a power of attorney; Rouget had not given any power before a notary since his arrival. If Rouget attempted to give one in Issoudun they would know instantly; if he left town to sign, they would discover it through registration. Therefore, if Rouget left, they would follow him and learn his movements. Philippe reported seeing his uncle cross the street and detailed his last visit. He told Hochon that Fario and a man named Benjamin Bourdet—a soldier Philippe had helped in Vatan—were watching; they would follow Rouget secretly. “If they let him walk with you,” Hochon mused, “Maxence must believe he has found some means to win the game.” Philippe said Maxence was afraid of him but could not evade him; Fario had stirred public censure against Maxence and the gossip was now in motion.

When Philippe left, Flore hurried to Max’s room with a full account of his boldness. Max resolved that before resorting to fighting him they would play a bold card. He told Flore to send at once to Père Cognette for a horse and a char-à-banc; pack their things and go to Vatan pretending to stay, taking Vedie and the twenty thousand francs in Rouget’s drawer. If Philippe brought Rouget to Vatan, Flore must refuse to return unless Rouget signed a power of attorney. As soon as they obtained it, Max would slip off to Paris while she came back to Issoudun. When Jean-Jacques found her gone he would be desperate and likely follow; Max planned to give him a stern talking-to when he did.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER XV

While the foregoing plot was progressing, Philippe was walking arm in
arm with his uncle along the boulevard Baron.

“The two great tacticians are coming to close quarters at last,” thought
Monsieur Hochon as he watched the colonel marching off with his uncle;
“I am curious to see the end of the game, and what becomes of the stake
of ninety thousand francs a year.”

“My dear uncle,” said Philippe, whose phraseology had a flavor of his
affinities in Paris, “you love this girl, and you are devilishly right.
She is damnably handsome! Instead of billing and cooing she makes you
trot like a valet; well, that’s simple enough; but she wants to see
you six feet underground, so that she may marry Max, whom she adores.”

“I know that, Philippe, but I love her all the same.”

“Well, I have sworn by the soul of my mother, who is your own sister,”
continued Philippe, “to make your Rabouilleuse as supple as my glove,
and the same as she was before that scoundrel, who is unworthy to have
served in the Imperial Guard, ever came to quarter himself in your
house.”

“Ah! if you could do that!--” said the old man.

“It is very easy,” answered Philippe, cutting his uncle short. “I’ll
kill Max as I would a dog; but--on one condition,” added the old
campaigner.

“What is that?” said Rouget, looking at his nephew in a stupid way.

“Don’t sign that power of attorney which they want of you before the
third of December; put them off till then. Your torturers only want it
to enable them to sell the fifty thousand a year you have in the Funds,
so that they may run off to Paris and pay for their wedding festivities
out of your millions.”

“I am afraid so,” replied Rouget.

“Well, whatever they may say or do to you, put off giving that power of
attorney until next week.”

“Yes; but when Flore talks to me she stirs my very soul, till I don’t
know what I do. I give you my word, when she looks at me in a certain
way, her blue eyes seem like paradise, and I am no longer master of
myself,--especially when for some days she had been harsh to me.”

“Well, whether she is sweet or sour, don’t do more than promise to sign
the paper, and let me know the night before you are going to do it. That
will answer. Maxence shall not be your proxy unless he first kills
me. If I kill him, you must agree to take me in his place, and I’ll
undertake to break in that handsome girl and keep her at your beck
and call. Yes, Flore shall love you, and if she doesn’t satisfy
you--thunder! I’ll thrash her.”

“Oh! I never could allow that. A blow struck at Flore would break my
heart.”

“But it is the only way to govern women and horses. A man makes himself
feared, or loved, or respected. Now that is what I wanted to whisper in
your ear--Good-morning, gentlemen,” he said to Mignonnet and Carpentier,
who came up at the moment; “I am taking my uncle for a walk, as you
see, and trying to improve him; for we are in an age when children are
obliged to educate their grandparents.”

They all bowed to each other.

“You behold in my dear uncle the effects of an unhappy passion. Those
two want to strip him of his fortune and leave him in the lurch--you
know to whom I refer? He sees the plot; but he hasn’t the courage to
give up his SUGAR-PLUM for a few days so as to baffle it.”

Philippe briefly explained his uncle’s position.

“Gentlemen,” he remarked, in conclusion, “you see there are no two ways
of saving him: either Colonel Bridau must kill Captain Gilet, or Captain
Gilet must kill Colonel Bridau. We celebrate the Emperor’s coronation
on the day after to-morrow; I rely upon you to arrange the seats at the
banquet so that I shall sit opposite to Gilet. You will do me the honor,
I hope, of being my seconds.”

“We will appoint you to preside, and sit ourselves on either side
of you. Max, as vice-president, will of course sit opposite,” said
Mignonnet.

“Oh! the scoundrel will have Potel and Renard with him,” said
Carpentier. “In spite of all that Issoudun now knows and says of his
midnight maraudings, those two worthy officers, who have already been
his seconds, remain faithful to him.”

“You see how it all maps out, uncle,” said Philippe. “Therefore, sign
no paper before the third of December; the next day you shall be free,
happy, and beloved by Flore, without having to coax for it.”

“You don’t know him, Philippe,” said the terrified old man. “Maxence has
killed nine men in duels.”

“Yes; but ninety thousand francs a year didn’t depend on it,” answered
Philippe.

“A bad conscience shakes the hand,” remarked Mignonnet sententiously.

“In a few days from now,” resumed Philippe, “you and the Rabouilleuse
will be living together as sweet as honey,--that is, after she gets
through mourning. At first she’ll twist like a worm, and yelp, and weep;
but never mind, let the water run!”

The two soldiers approved of Philippe’s arguments, and tried to hearten
up old Rouget, with whom they walked about for nearly two hours. At last
Philippe took his uncle home, saying as they parted:--

“Don’t take any steps without me. I know women. I have paid for one, who
cost me far more than Flore can ever cost you. But she taught me how to
behave to the fair sex for the rest of my days. Women are bad children;
they are inferior animals to men; we must make them fear us; the worst
condition in the world is to be governed by such brutes.”

It was about half-past two in the afternoon when the old man got home.
Kouski opened the door in tears,--that is, by Max’s orders, he gave
signs of weeping.

“Oh! Monsieur, Madame has gone away, and taken Vedie with her!”

“Gone--a--way!” said the old man in a strangled voice.

The blow was so violent that Rouget sat down on the stairs, unable to
stand. A moment after, he rose, looked about the hall, into the kitchen,
went up to his own room, searched all the chambers, and returned to the
salon, where he threw himself into a chair, and burst into tears.

“Where is she?” he sobbed. “Oh! where is she? where is Max?”

“I don’t know,” answered Kouski. “The captain went out without telling
me.”

Gilet thought it politic to be seen sauntering about the town. By
leaving the old man alone with his despair, he knew he should make him
feel his desertion the more keenly, and reduce him to docility. To keep
Philippe from assisting his uncle at this crisis, he had given Kouski
strict orders not to open the door to any one. Flore away, the miserable
old man grew frantic, and the situation of things approached a crisis.
During his walk through the town, Maxence Gilet was avoided by many
persons who a day or two earlier would have hastened to shake hands with
him. A general reaction had set in against him. The deeds of the Knights
of Idleness were ringing on every tongue. The tale of Joseph Bridau’s
arrest, now cleared up, disgraced Max in the eyes of all; and his life
and conduct received in one day their just award. Gilet met Captain
Potel, who was looking for him, and seemed almost beside himself.

“What’s the matter with you, Potel?”

“My dear fellow, the Imperial Guard is being black-guarded all over the
town! These civilians are crying you down! and it goes to the bottom of
my heart.”

“What are they complaining of?” asked Max.

“Of what you do at night.”

“As if we couldn’t amuse ourselves a little!”

“But that isn’t all,” said Potel.

Potel belonged to the same class as the officer who replied to the
burgomasters: “Eh! your town will be paid for, if we do burn it!” So he
was very little troubled about the deeds of the Order of Idleness.

“What more?” inquired Gilet.

“The Guard is against the Guard. It is that that breaks my heart. Bridau
has set all these bourgeois on you. The Guard against the Guard! no, it
ought not to be! You can’t back down, Max; you must meet Bridau. I had a
great mind to pick a quarrel with the low scoundrel myself and send him
to the shades; I wish I had, and then the bourgeois wouldn’t have seen
the spectacle of the Guard against the Guard. In war times, I don’t
say anything against it. Two heroes of the Guard may quarrel, and
fight,--but at least there are no civilians to look on and sneer. No, I
say that big villain never served in the Guard. A guardsman would never
behave as he does to another guardsman, under the very eyes of the
bourgeois; impossible! Ah! it’s all wrong; the Guard is disgraced--and
here, at Issoudun! where it was once so honored.”

“Come, Potel, don’t worry yourself,” answered Max; “even if you do not
see me at the banquet--”

“What! do you mean that you won’t be there the day after to-morrow?”
cried Potel, interrupting his friend. “Do you wish to be called a
coward? and have it said you are running away from Bridau? No, no! The
unmounted grenadiers of the Guard can not draw back before the dragoons
of the Guard. Arrange your business in some other way and be there!”

“One more to send to the shades!” said Max. “Well, I think I can manage
my business so as to get there--For,” he thought to himself, “that power
of attorney ought not to be in my name; as old Heron says, it would look
too much like theft.”

This lion, tangled in the meshes Philippe Bridau was weaving for him,
muttered between his teeth as he went along; he avoided the looks of
those he met and returned home by the boulevard Vilatte, still talking
to himself.

“I will have that money before I fight,” he said. “If I die, it shall
not go to Philippe. I must put it in Flore’s name. She will follow my
instructions, and go straight to Paris. Once there, she can marry, if
she chooses, the son of some marshal of France who has been sent to the
right-about. I’ll have that power of attorney made in Baruch’s name, and
he’ll transfer the property by my order.”

Max, to do him justice, was never more cool and calm in appearance
than when his blood and his ideas were boiling. No man ever united in a
higher degree the qualities which make a great general. If his career
had not been cut short by his captivity at Cabrera, the Emperor would
certainly have found him one of those men who are necessary to the
success of vast enterprises. When he entered the room where the hapless
victim of all these comic and tragic scenes was still weeping, Max asked
the meaning of such distress; seemed surprised, pretended that he knew
nothing, and heard, with well-acted amazement, of Flore’s departure.
He questioned Kouski, to obtain some light on the object of this
inexplicable journey.

“Madame said like this,” Kouski replied, “--that I was to tell monsieur
she had taken twenty thousand francs in gold from his drawer, thinking
that monsieur wouldn’t refuse her that amount as wages for the last
twenty-two years.”

“Wages?” exclaimed Rouget.

“Yes,” replied Kouski. “Ah! I shall never come back,” she said to
Vedie as she drove away. “Poor Vedie, who is so attached to monsieur,
remonstrated with madame. ‘No, no,’ she answered, ‘he has no affection
for me; he lets his nephew treat me like the lowest of the low’; and she
wept--oh! bitterly.”

“Eh! what do I care for Philippe?” cried the old man, whom Max was
watching. “Where is Flore? how can we find out where she is?”

“Philippe, whose advice you follow, will help you,” said Max coldly.

“Philippe?” said the old man, “what has he to do with the poor child?
There is no one but you, my good Max, who can find Flore. She will
follow you--you could bring her back to me--”

“I don’t wish to oppose Monsieur Bridau,” observed Max.

“As for that,” cried Rouget, “if that hinders you, he told me he meant
to kill you.”

“Ah!” exclaimed Gilet, laughing, “we will see about it!”

“My friend,” said the old man, “find Flore, and I will do all she wants
of me.”

“Some one must have seen her as she passed through the town,” said
Maxence to Kouski. “Serve dinner; put everything on the table, and then
go and make inquiries from place to place. Let us know, by dessert,
which road Mademoiselle Brazier has taken.”

This order quieted for a time the poor creature, who was moaning like
