a child that has lost its nurse. At this moment Rouget, who hated Max,
thought his tormentor an angel. A passion like that of this miserable
old man for Flore is astonishingly like the emotions of childhood. At
six o’clock, the Pole, who had merely taken a walk, returned to announce
that Flore had driven towards Vatan.

“Madame is going back to her own people, that’s plain,” said Kouski.

“Would you like to go to Vatan to-night?” said Max. “The road is
bad, but Kouski knows how to drive, and you’ll make your peace better
to-night than to-morrow morning.”

“Let us go!” cried Rouget.

“Put the horse in quietly,” said Max to Kouski; “manage, if you can,
that the town shall not know of this nonsense, for Monsieur Rouget’s
sake. Saddle my horse,” he added in a whisper. “I will ride on ahead of
you.”

Monsieur Hochon had already notified Philippe of Flore’s departure; and
the colonel rose from Monsieur Mignonnet’s dinner-table to rush to the
place Saint-Jean; for he at once guessed the meaning of this clever
strategy. When Philippe presented himself at his uncle’s house, Kouski
answered through a window that Monsieur Rouget was unable to see any
one.

“Fario,” said Philippe to the Spaniard, who was stationed in the
Grande-Narette, “go and tell Benjamin to mount his horse; it is
all-important that I shall know what Gilet does with my uncle.”

“They are now putting the horse into the caleche,” said Fario, who had
been watching the Rouget stable.

“If they go towards Vatan,” answered Philippe, “get me another horse,
and come yourself with Benjamin to Monsieur Mignonnet’s.”

“What do you mean to do?” asked Monsieur Hochon, who had come out of his
own house when he saw Philippe and Fario standing together.

“The genius of a general, my dear Monsieur Hochon,” said Philippe,
“consists not only in carefully observing the enemy’s movements, but
also in guessing his intentions from those movements, and in modifying
his own plan whenever the enemy interferes with it by some unexpected
action. Now, if my uncle and Max drive out together, they are going
to Vatan; Maxence will have promised to reconcile him with Flore, who
‘fugit ad salices,’--the manoeuvre is General Virgil’s. If that’s the
line they take, I don’t yet know what I shall do; I shall have some
hours to think it over, for my uncle can’t sign a power of attorney at
ten o’clock at night; the notaries will all be in bed. If, as I
rather fancy, Max goes on in advance of my uncle to teach Flore her
lesson,--which seems necessary and probable,--the rogue is lost! you
will see the sort of revenge we old soldiers take in a game of this
kind. Now, as I need a helper for this last stroke, I must go back to
Mignonnet’s and make an arrangement with my friend Carpentier.”

Shaking hands with Monsieur Hochon, Philippe went off down the
Petite-Narette to Mignonnet’s house. Ten minutes later, Monsieur Hochon
saw Max ride off at a quick trot; and the old miser’s curiosity was
so powerfully excited that he remained standing at his window, eagerly
expecting to hear the wheels of the old demi-fortune, which was not long
in coming. Jean-Jacques’s impatience made him follow Max within twenty
minutes. Kouski, no doubt under orders from his master, walked the horse
through the town.

“If they get to Paris, all is lost,” thought Monsieur Hochon.

At this moment, a lad from the faubourg de Rome came to the Hochon house
with a letter for Baruch. The two grandsons, much subdued by the events
of the morning, had kept their rooms of their own accord during the day.
Thinking over their prospects, they saw plainly that they had better be
cautious with their grandparents. Baruch knew very well the influence
which his grandfather Hochon exerted over his grandfather and
grandmother Borniche: Monsieur Hochon would not hesitate to get their
property for Adolphine if his conduct were such as to make them pin
their hopes on the grand marriage with which his grandfather had
threatened him that morning. Being richer than Francois, Baruch had the
most to lose; he therefore counselled an absolute surrender, with no
other condition than the payment of their debt to Max. As for Francois,
his future was entirely in the hands of his grandfather; he had no
expectations except from him, and by the guardianship account, he was
now his debtor. The two young men accordingly gave solemn promises
of amendment, prompted by their imperilled interests, and by the hope
Madame Hochon held out, that the debt to Max should be paid.

“You have done very wrong,” she said to them; “repair it by future good
conduct, and Monsieur Hochon will forget it.”

So, when Francois had read the letter which had been brought for Baruch,
over the latter’s shoulder, he whispered in his ear, “Ask grandpapa’s
advice.”

“Read this,” said Baruch, taking the letter to old Hochon.

“Read it to me yourself; I haven’t my spectacles.”

  My dear Friend,--I hope you will not hesitate, under the serious
  circumstances in which I find myself, to do me the service of
  receiving a power of attorney from Monsieur Rouget. Be at Vatan
  to-morrow morning at nine o’clock. I shall probably send you to
  Paris, but don’t be uneasy; I will furnish you with money for the
  journey, and join you there immediately. I am almost sure I shall
  be obliged to leave Issoudun, December third.

  Adieu. I count on your friendship; rely on that of your friend,

  Maxence


“God be praised!” exclaimed Monsieur Hochon; “the property of that old
idiot is saved from the claws of the devil.”

“It will be if you say so,” said Madame Hochon; “and I thank God,--who
has no doubt heard my prayers. The prosperity of the wicked is always
fleeting.”

“You must go to Vatan, and accept the power of attorney from Monsieur
Rouget,” said the old man to Baruch. “Their object is to get fifty
thousand francs a year transferred to Mademoiselle Brazier. They will
send you to Paris, and you must seem to go; but you are to stop at
Orleans, and wait there till you hear from me. Let no one--not a
soul--know where you lodge; go to the first inn you come to in the
faubourg Bannier, no matter if it is only a post-house--”

“Look here!” cried Francois, who had rushed to the window at the sudden
noise of wheels in the Grande-Narette. “Here’s something new!--Pere
Rouget and Colonel Bridau coming back together in the caleche, Benjamin
and Captain Carpentier following on horseback!”

“I’ll go over,” cried Monsieur Hochon, whose curiosity carried the day
over every other feeling.

Monsieur Hochon found old Rouget in his bedroom, writing the following
letter at his nephew’s dictation:

  Mademoiselle,--If you do not start to return here the moment you
  receive this letter, your conduct will show such ingratitude for
  all my goodness that I shall revoke the will I have made in your
  favor, and give my property to my nephew Philippe. You will
  understand that Monsieur Gilet can no longer be my guest after
  staying with you at Vatan. I send this letter by Captain
  Carpentier, who will put it into your own hands. I hope you will
  listen to his advice; he will speak to you with authority from me.
  Your affectionate

  J.-J. Rouget.


“Captain Carpentier and I MET my uncle, who was so foolish as to follow
Mademoiselle Brazier and Monsieur Gilet to Vatan,” said Philippe, with
sarcastic emphasis, to Monsieur Hochon. “I have made my uncle see that
he was running his head into a noose; for that girl will abandon him the
moment she gets him to sign a power of attorney, by which they mean to
obtain the income of his money in the Funds. That letter will bring
her back under his roof, the handsome runaway! this very night, or I’m
mistaken. I promise to make her as pliable as a bit of whalebone for the
rest of her days, if my uncle allows me to take Maxence Gilet’s place;
which, in my opinion, he ought never to have had in the first place. Am
I not right?--and yet here’s my uncle bemoaning himself!”

“Neighbor,” said Monsieur Hochon, “you have taken the best means to get
peace in your household. Destroy your will, and Flore will be once more
what she used to be in the early days.”

“No, she will never forgive me for what I have made her suffer,”
whimpered the old man; “she will no longer love me.”

“She shall love you, and closely too; I’ll take care of that,” said
Philippe.

“Come, open your eyes!” exclaimed Monsieur Hochon. “They mean to rob you
and abandon you.”

“Oh! I was sure of it!” cried the poor imbecile.

“See, here is a letter Maxence has written to my grandson Borniche,”
said old Hochon. “Read it.”

“What infamy!” exclaimed Carpentier, as he listened to the letter, which
Rouget read aloud, weeping.

“Is that plain enough, uncle?” demanded Philippe. “Hold that hussy by
her interests and she’ll adore you as you deserve.”

“She loves Maxence too well; she will leave me,” cried the frightened
old man.

“But, uncle, Maxence or I,--one or the other of us--won’t leave our
footsteps in the dust of Issoudun three days hence.”

“Well then go, Monsieur Carpentier,” said Rouget; “if you promise me to
bring her back, go! You are a good man; say to her in my name all you
think you ought to say.”

“Captain Carpentier will whisper in her ear that I have sent to Paris
for a woman whose youth and beauty are captivating; that will bring the
jade back in a hurry!”

The captain departed, driving himself in the old caleche; Benjamin
accompanied him on horseback, for Kouski was nowhere to be found. Though
threatened by the officers with arrest and the loss of his situation,
the Pole had gone to Vatan on a hired horse, to warn Max and Flore of
the adversary’s move. After fulfilling his mission, Carpentier, who did
not wish to drive back with Flore, was to change places with Benjamin,
and take the latter’s horse.

When Philippe was told of Kouski’s flight he said to Benjamin, “You will
take the Pole’s place, from this time on. It is all mapping out, papa
Hochon!” cried the lieutenant-colonel. “That banquet will be jovial!”

“You will come and live here, of course,” said the old miser.

“I have told Fario to send me all my things,” answered Philippe. “I
shall sleep in the room adjoining Gilet’s apartment,--if my uncle
consents.”

“What will come of all this?” cried the terrified old man.

“Mademoiselle Flore Brazier is coming, gentle as a paschal lamb,”
replied Monsieur Hochon.

“God grant it!” exclaimed Rouget, wiping his eyes.

“It is now seven o’clock,” said Philippe; “the sovereign of your heart
will be here at half-past eleven: you’ll never see Gilet again, and you
will be as happy ever after as a pope.--If you want me to succeed,” he
whispered to Monsieur Hochon, “stay here till the hussy comes; you can
help me in keeping the old man up to his resolution; and, together,
we’ll make that crab-girl see on which side her bread is buttered.”

Monsieur Hochon felt the reasonableness of the request and stayed:
but they had their hands full, for old Rouget gave way to childish
lamentations, which were only quieted by Philippe’s repeating over and
over a dozen times:--

“Uncle, you will see that I am right when Flore returns to you as tender
as ever. You shall be petted; you will save your property: be guided by
my advice, and you’ll live in paradise for the rest of your days.”

When, about half-past eleven, wheels were heard in the Grande-Narette,
the question was, whether the carriage were returning full or empty.
Rouget’s face wore an expression of agony, which changed to the
prostration of excessive joy when he saw the two women, as the carriage
turned to enter the courtyard.

“Kouski,” said Philippe, giving a hand to Flore to help her down. “You
are no longer in Monsieur Rouget’s service. You will not sleep here
to-night; get your things together, and go. Benjamin takes your place.”

“Are you the master here?” said Flore sarcastically.

“With your permission,” replied Philippe, squeezing her hand as if in a
vice. “Come! we must have an understanding, you and I”; and he led the
bewildered woman out into the place Saint-Jean.

“My fine lady,” began the old campaigner, stretching out his right hand,
“three days hence, Maxence Gilet will be sent to the shades by that arm,
or his will have taken me off guard. If I die, you will be the mistress
of my poor imbecile uncle; ‘bene sit.’ If I remain on my pins, you’ll
have to walk straight, and keep him supplied with first-class happiness.
If you don’t, I know girls in Paris who are, with all due respect, much
prettier than you; for they are only seventeen years old: they would
make my uncle excessively happy, and they are in my interests. Begin
your attentions this very evening; if the old man is not as gay as a
lark to-morrow morning, I have only a word to say to you; it is this,
pay attention to it,--there is but one way to kill a man without the
interference of the law, and that is to fight a duel with him; but I
know three ways to get rid of a woman: mind that, my beauty!”

During this address, Flore shook like a person with the ague.

“Kill Max--?” she said, gazing at Philippe in the moonlight.

“Come, here’s my uncle.”

Old Rouget, turning a deaf ear to Monsieur Hochon’s remonstrances, now
came out into the street, and took Flore by the hand, as a miser might
have grasped his treasure; he drew her back to the house and into his
own room and shut the door.

“This is Saint-Lambert’s day, and he who deserts his place, loses it,”
remarked Benjamin to the Pole.

“My master will shut your mouth for you,” answered Kouski, departing to
join Max who established himself at the hotel de la Poste.

On the morrow, between nine and eleven o’clock, all the women talked
to each other from door to door throughout the town. The story of the
wonderful change in the Rouget household spread everywhere. The upshot
of the conversations was the same on all sides,--

“What will happen at the banquet between Max and Colonel Bridau?”

Philippe said but few words to the Vedie,--“Six hundred francs’ annuity,
or dismissal.” They were enough, however, to keep her neutral, for a
time, between the two great powers, Philippe and Flore.

Knowing Max’s life to be in danger, Flore became more affectionate
to Rouget than in the first days of their alliance. Alas! in love, a
self-interested devotion is sometimes more agreeable than a truthful
one; and that is why many men pay so much for clever deceivers. The
Rabouilleuse did not appear till the next morning, when she came down to
breakfast with Rouget on her arm. Tears filled her eyes as she beheld,
sitting in Max’s place, the terrible adversary, with his sombre blue
eyes, and the cold, sinister expression on his face.

“What is the matter, mademoiselle?” he said, after wishing his uncle
good-morning.

“She can’t endure the idea of your fighting Maxence,” said old Rouget.

“I have not the slightest desire to kill Gilet,” answered Philippe. “He
need only take himself off from Issoudun and go to America on a venture.
I should be the first to advise you to give him an outfit, and to wish
him a safe voyage. He would soon make a fortune there, and that is far
more honorable than turning Issoudun topsy-turvy at night, and playing
the devil in your household.”

“Well, that’s fair enough,” said Rouget, glancing at Flore.

“A-mer-i-ca!” she ejaculated, sobbing.

“It is better to kick his legs about in a free country than have them
rot in a pine box in France. However, perhaps you think he is a good
shot, and can kill me; it’s on the cards,” observed the colonel.

“Will you let me speak to him?” said Flore, imploring Philippe in a
humble and submissive tone.

“Certainly; he can come here and pack up his things. I will stay with
my uncle during that time; for I shall not leave the old man again,”
replied Philippe.

“Vedie,” cried Flore, “run to the hotel, and tell Monsieur Gilet that I
beg him--”

“--to come and get his belongings,” said Philippe, interrupting Flore’s
message.

“Yes, yes, Vedie; that will be a good pretext to see me; I must speak to
him.”

Terror controlled her hatred; and the shock which her whole being
experienced when she first encountered this strong and pitiless nature
was now so overwhelming that she bowed before Philippe just as Rouget
had been in the habit of bending before her. She anxiously awaited
Vedie’s return. The woman brought a formal refusal from Max, who
requested Mademoiselle Brazier to send his things to the hotel de la
Poste.

“Will you allow me to take them to him?” she said to Jean-Jacques
Rouget.

“Yes, but will you come back?” said the old man.

“If Mademoiselle is not back by midday, you will give me a power of
attorney to attend to your property,” said Philippe, looking at Flore.
“Take Vedie with you, to save appearances, mademoiselle. In future you
are to think of my uncle’s honor.”

Flore could get nothing out of Max. Desperate at having allowed himself,
before the eyes of the whole town, to be routed out of his shameless
position, Gilet was too proud to run away from Philippe. The
Rabouilleuse combated this objection, and proposed that they should fly
together to America; but Max, who did not want Flore without her money,
and yet did not wish the girl to see the bottom of his heart, insisted
on his intention of killing Philippe.

“We have committed a monstrous folly,” he said. “We ought all three to
have gone to Paris and spent the winter there; but how could one guess,
from the mere sight of that fellow’s big carcass, that things would turn
out as they have? The turn of events is enough to make one giddy! I took
the colonel for one of those fire-eaters who haven’t two ideas in their
head; that was the blunder I made. As I didn’t have the sense to double
like a hare in the beginning, I’ll not be such a coward as to back down
before him. He has lowered me in the estimation of this town, and I
cannot get back what I have lost unless I kill him.”

“Go to America with forty thousand francs. I’ll find a way to get rid of
that scoundrel, and join you. It would be much wiser.”

“What would people say of me?” he exclaimed. “No; I have buried nine
already. The fellow doesn’t seem as if he knew much; he went from school
to the army, and there he was always fighting till 1815; then he went
to America, and I doubt if the brute ever set foot in a fencing-alley;
while I have no match with the sabre. The sabre is his arm; I shall seem
very generous in offering it to him,--for I mean, if possible, to let
him insult me,--and I can easily run him through. Unquestionably, it is
my wisest course. Don’t be uneasy; we shall be masters of the field in a
couple of days.”

That it was that a stupid point of honor had more influence over Max
than sound policy. When Flore got home she shut herself up to cry at
ease. During the whole of that day gossip ran wild in Issoudun, and the
duel between Philippe and Maxence was considered inevitable.

“Ah! Monsieur Hochon,” said Mignonnet, who, accompanied by Carpentier,
met the old man on the boulevard Baron, “we are very uneasy; for Gilet
is clever with all weapons.”

“Never mind,” said the old provincial diplomatist; “Philippe has managed
this thing well from the beginning. I should never have thought that
big, easy-going fellow would have succeeded as he has. The two have
rolled together like a couple of thunder-clouds.”

“Oh!” said Carpentier, “Philippe is a remarkable man. His conduct before
the Court of Peers was a masterpiece of diplomacy.”

“Well, Captain Renard,” said one of the townsfolk to Max’s friend. “They
say wolves don’t devour each other, but it seems that Max is going
to set his teeth in Colonel Bridau. That’s pretty serious among you
gentlemen of the Old Guard.”

“You make fun of it, do you? Because the poor fellow amused himself a
little at night, you are all against him,” said Potel. “But Gilet is a
man who couldn’t stay in a hole like Issoudun without finding something
to do.”

“Well, gentlemen,” remarked another, “Max and the colonel must play out
their game. Bridau had to avenge his brother. Don’t you remember Max’s
treachery to the poor lad?”

“Bah! nothing but an artist,” said Renard.

“But the real question is about the old man’s property,” said a third.
“They say Monsieur Gilet was laying hands on fifty thousand francs a
year, when the colonel turned him out of his uncle’s house.”

“Gilet rob a man! Come, don’t say that to any one but me, Monsieur
Canivet,” cried Potel. “If you do, I’ll make you swallow your
tongue,--and without any sauce.”

Every household in town offered prayers for the honorable Colonel
Bridau.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER XVI

Towards four o’clock the following day, the officers of the old army at Issoudun and its environs were sauntering about the place du Marché, in front of an eating-house kept by Lacroix, waiting the arrival of Colonel Philippe Bridau. The banquet in honor of the coronation was to begin at five. Various groups discussed Max’s discomfiture and his dismissal from old Rouget’s house; the officers were to dine at Lacroix’s, and the common soldiers planned a meeting at a neighboring wine-shop. Among the officers, Potel and Renard alone defended Max.

“Is it any of our business what takes place among the old man’s heirs?” said Renard.

“Max is weak with women,” remarked Potel.

“There’ll be sabres unsheathed before long,” said an old sub-lieutenant.

“A folly that doesn’t succeed becomes a crime,” Mignonnet observed dryly.

Max joined the old soldiers and was received in significant silence. Potel and Renard took his arms and walked with him, conversing. Presently Philippe approached in full dress; he carried himself with an air that contrasted with the forced attention Max paid his two supporters. Bridau’s hand was grasped by Mignonnet, Carpentier, and several others. This welcome, so different from that accorded Max, dispelled the last feeling of cowardice or prudence awakened in him by Flore’s entreaties and tendernesses.

“We shall fight,” he said to Renard, “and to the death. Therefore don’t talk to me any more; let me play my part well.”

The three Bonapartists returned to the group of officers. Max bowed first to Bridau, who returned the bow; the two exchanged a frigid glance.

“Come, gentlemen, let us take our seats,” said Potel.

“And drink to the health of the Little Corporal, who is now in the paradise of heroes,” cried Renard.

The company entered Lacroix’s long, low dining-hall. At Philippe’s request, the two adversaries were seated directly opposite each other. Some young men of the town, among them Knights of Idleness, lingered outside, anxious to see what might happen. They deplored the crisis, though each considered a duel inevitable.

Throughout the meal both antagonists displayed vigilance beneath apparent joviality. At dessert Philippe rose and proposed the first toast.

“To the Grand Army!” he cried.

“To the Grand Army!” repeated every voice. Eleven private soldiers, including Benjamin and Kouski, then entered and repeated the toast. The old soldiers came in and stood behind the officers.

“I claim the second toast,” said Mignonnet. “Let us drink to those who attempted to restore his son!”

Everyone present, except Maxence Gilet, bowed to Philippe and raised their glasses. Max rose and, amid expectancy, bowed ironically to Philippe.

“May we all meet again at this time next year,” said Max.

His voice, though leveled, was loaded; some muttered “It’s coming!” and Kouski whispered predictions. Tension lingered, but the two antagonists spoke in tones of ordinary conversation; there was nothing theatrical except the profound stillness.

“Look here, you others!” said Philippe to the soldiers; “our affairs don’t concern the bourgeoisie—no word, therefore. It is for the Old Guard only.”

“They’ll obey orders, colonel,” Renard replied.

The dessert passed; libations were plentiful. The antagonists and their four seconds arranged that a duel involving such fortune and reputations should not appear an ordinary brawl. Both behaved like gentlemen, calming the anxious crowd. At ten o’clock that night the two adversaries agreed on sabres; the rendezvous was behind the chancel of the Capuchins’ church at eight o’clock next morning. Goddet, former army surgeon, would be present. The seconds agreed the combat would last only ten minutes, whatever occurred.

At eleven that night Monsieur and Madame Hochon surprised Colonel Bridau in his rooms. Madame Hochon, tearful, implored him to say his prayers before leaving the house and presented him a relic: a tooth of Sainte Solange mounted on black velvet. She urged him to wear it.

“Will it protect me from a sabre-thrust?” asked Philippe.

“Yes,” she replied.

“Then I have no right to wear that accoutrement any more than if it were a cuirass,” cried Philippe.

The old lady kissed his forehead and gave thirty francs—her all—to Benjamin, asking him to sew the relic into Philippe’s trousers. Benjamin complied, not from belief in its virtue but because the pay and the commission constrained him. Madame Hochon left confident in Sainte Solange.

At eight the next morning, December third, with cloudy weather, Max, his seconds, and the Pole arrived on the little meadow around the apse of the Capuchins’ church. There Philippe and his seconds waited, Benjamin among them. Potel and Mignonnet paced off twenty-four feet; the attendants drew lines with a spade at each end: no man was allowed to retreat beyond his line under pain of dishonor. Each was to stand at his line and advance when the seconds gave the word.

“Do we take off our coats?” asked Philippe coldly.

“Of course,” answered Maxence, adopting a bully’s manner.

They stripped to shirts; both men’s muscles hardly trembled despite the cold. Goddet, the seconds, and two soldiers admired their composure.

Just as the signal was given, Max caught sight of Fario’s sinister face peering through a hole in the church roof. Those eyes, full of hatred and revenge, for a moment dazzled him. Philippe adopted the inside guard that marks a duellist of the first rank. Max sensed inferiority.

“Ah! the lascar!” he thought; “he’s an expert; I’m lost!”

He attempted a moulinet, twirling his sabre to bewilder Philippe and disarm him; but at the first encounter he felt the colonel’s wrist was iron and spring-like. Philippe’s parries were cool and precise. Between two men of such calibre, victory depends on a flash of calculation and a single false movement. At a bad parry by Max the colonel sent the sabre spinning from his hand.

“Pick it up,” he said; “I am not the man to kill a disarmed enemy.”

There was something atrocious in the grandeur of these words; they seemed to show such conscious superiority that the onlookers took them for calculation. When Max resumed position, he had lost coolness and confronted again Philippe’s raised guard. He resolved to redeem his shame by a bold stroke; taking his sabre in both hands he rushed furiously, determined to kill or be killed. Philippe received a cut slashing his forehead and part of his face, but with a terrible oblique moulinet he cleft Max’s head. These two savage blows ended the combat in the ninth minute. Fario came down to gloat over his enemy in convulsions; Philippe was carried back to his uncle’s house.

Thus perished a man who might have done great deeds in different surroundings; nature had given him courage, self-possession, and political sagacity, but not the nobility of conduct needed for greatness. He was not much regretted, given the perfidy by which his adversary—worse in character—had brought him into disrepute. His death ended the exploits of the Order of Idleness, to Issoudun’s relief. Philippe had little to fear as the duel’s circumstances were spread with praise for the bravery of both combatants.

“But they had better both have been killed,” remarked Monsieur Mouilleron; “it would have been a good riddance for the Government.”

Flore Brazier’s situation would have been embarrassing but for the condition into which she fell after Max’s death. A brain fever set in, combined with dangerous inflammation from her escapade to Vatan. If she had had ordinary health she might have fled the house where Max’s bed lay and where his murderer suffered; instead she hovered between life and death for three months, attended by Goddet, who also attended Philippe.

As soon as Philippe could write he sent letters.

To Monsieur Desroches he reported he had “killed the most venomous of the two reptiles,” though he himself had his head split by a sabre. He noted that the other “viper” remained and that he must come to terms with her because his uncle clung to her. An illness had kept Flore in bed, which he hoped Providence would use to call her soul if she repented. By his account Goddet—“a worthy soul”—and others had been won to his side; Goddet preached resignation and religion to Flore and had promised to keep her in bed for three months. He had bought over the cook, who spread tales suggesting Max would have ill-treated Flore. “All goes well so far,” he wrote; “my uncle, by old Hochon’s advice, has destroyed his will.”

To Monsieur Giroudeau, care of Mademoiselle Florentine, he requested a girl—Cesarine, if available—or some ballet-girl who could appear as the respectable daughter of a brave soldier and stand ready to come at his call; if she answered his purpose he would give her fifty thousand francs on his uncle’s death. He told of his duel and hinted at lively times to come.

Agathe, informed by Madame Hochon, rushed to Issoudun and was given Philippe’s former room. Her maternal tenderness revived as she listened to town praises for her son.

“After all, my child,” said Madame Hochon, “youth must have its fling. The dissipations of a soldier under the Empire must, of course, be greater than those of young men looked after by their fathers. Thanks to your son, Issoudun now breathes and sleeps in peace. Philippe has come to his senses rather late; he told us frankly that those three months in the Luxembourg sobered him. Monsieur Hochon is delighted with his conduct here; everyone thinks highly of it. If he can be kept away from the temptations of Paris, he will end by being a comfort to you.”

Philippe played the saint to his mother because he needed her. He did not wish to resort to Cesarine unless he continued to be an object of horror to Mademoiselle Brazier. He recognized Flore was essential to his uncle’s life and preferred to use her rather than bring in a dancer who might marry the old man. He conceived the idea of making his uncle marry Flore, thus legitimizing her position and keeping her—and the property—within reach. He asked his mother to visit Flore and treat her in a sisterly way.

“I must confess, my dear mother,” he said in an affected tone before Monsieur and Madame Hochon, “that my uncle’s way of life is not becoming; he could, however, make Mademoiselle Brazier respected by the community if he chose. Wouldn’t it be far better for her to be Madame Rouget than the servant-mistress of an old bachelor? She had better obtain a definite right to his property by a marriage contract. If you, or Monsieur Hochon, or some good priest would speak to both parties, you might put a stop to the scandal which offends decent people.”

The next day Agathe and Madame Hochon appeared at Flore’s bedside and repeated Philippe’s words. In Issoudun the colonel was praised as a man of noble character, particularly because of his conduct toward Flore. For a month Flore heard Goddet, Madame Hochon, Agathe, and Rouget all urging a marriage. When, attracted by the idea of becoming Madame Rouget, she grew eager to recover so the ceremony could occur, it was not difficult to make her understand that she would be unwelcome in the Rouget family if she intended to turn Philippe out.

“Besides,” the doctor whispered, “you really owe him this good fortune. Max would never have allowed you to marry old Rouget. And, if you have children, you can revenge Max, for that will disinherit the Bridaus.”

Two months after the duel, in February 1823, the sick woman, urged by those about her and implored by Rouget, consented to receive Philippe; the sight of his scars moved her to tears, and his softened manner calmed her. By Philippe’s wish they were left alone.

“My dear child,” he said, “it is I who, from the start, have advised your marriage with my uncle; if you consent, it will take place as soon as you are recovered.”

“So they tell me,” she replied.

“Circumstances have compelled me to give you pain; it is natural therefore that I should wish to do you all the good I can. Wealth, respect, and a family position are worth more than what you have lost. You wouldn’t have been that fellow’s wife long after my uncle’s death, for I happen to know, through friends of his, that he intended to get rid of you. Come, my dear, let us understand each other and live happily. You shall be my aunt and nothing more. You will take care that my uncle does not forget me in his will; on my side, you shall see how well I will have you treated in the marriage contract. Keep calm, think it over, and we will talk later. All sensible people, indeed the whole town, urge you to put an end to your illegal position; no one will blame you for receiving me. It is well understood in the world that interests go before feelings. By the day of your marriage you will be handsomer than ever. The pallor of illness has given you an air of distinction, and on my honor, if my uncle did not love you so madly, you should be the wife of Colonel Bridau.”

Philippe left after dropping into Flore’s mind the idea of revenge through children—a hint that stirred her. In this scene Philippe echoed, in miniature, the manipulative speech of a Richard or a schemer: personal calculation, masked as sentiment, has formidable power over the heart. Nature herself works by self-interest.

At the beginning of April 1823, Rouget’s hall hosted a splendid dinner to celebrate the signing of the marriage contract between Mademoiselle Flore Brazier and the old bachelor. Guests included Monsieur Heron, the witnesses, Mignonnet, Carpentier, Hochon, Goddet, the mayor, the curate, Agathe Bridau, Madame Hochon and her friend Madame Borniche. The bride was much impressed by the protection offered by Philippe and the two ladies. Flore was dazzling. The curate, who had been instructing her for the last fortnight, was to allow her first communion the following day. The local journals praised the marriage as a revival of religion and a moral correction; the “Journal du Cher” and the “Journal de l’Indre” printed accounts that lauded Philippe’s supposed disinterestedness.

By the contract Rouget secured to Flore a dower of one hundred thousand francs and a life annuity of thirty thousand.

After the wedding, Agathe returned to Paris triumphant and told Joseph and Desroches the “good news.”

“Your son Philippe is too wily a man not to keep his paw on that inheritance,” said Desroches.

“You, and Joseph too, will always be unjust to that poor boy,” said Madame Bridau. She defended her son’s conduct as statesmanlike, a reaction to idle powers misapplied.

“Why do you suppose that he will not succeed by honest means?” she demanded.

“You will see!” exclaimed Desroches. “Fortunate or unfortunate, Philippe will remain the man of the Rue Mazarin, the murderer of Madame Descoings, the domestic thief. But don’t worry yourself; he will manage to appear honest to the world.”

The morning after the marriage, Philippe took Madame Rouget aside.

“My dear aunt,” he said, “you now belong to the family. Thanks to me, the law has tied the knot. Now, no nonsense. I intend that you and I should play above board. I know the tricks you will try against me; and I shall watch you like a duenna. You will never go out of this house except on my arm; and you will never leave me. As to what passes within the house, damn it, you’ll find me like a spider in the middle of his web. Here is something,” he continued, showing her a letter, “which will prove to you that I could, while you were lying ill upstairs, have turned you out of doors without a penny. Read it.”

The letter was from the theatrical set—Mariette and friends—detailing a plan to provide a trained actress to entrap Rouget and secure the property for Philippe. It described the girl’s arts and the social circle prepared to exploit any fortune, and suggested the pleasures awaiting Philippe in Paris. The letter shook Madame Rouget; terror and dependency betrayed her.

“I trust you,” he said, fixing her with terrible meaning, “as you see; but I expect some return. I have made you my aunt intending to marry you some day. You are worth more to me than Esther in managing my uncle. In a year from now we must be in Paris; you will amuse yourself much better there than here; it is a perpetual carnival. I shall return to the army and become a general, and you will be a great lady. There’s our future; now work for it. But I must have a pledge to bind this agreement. You are to give me, within a month from now, a power of attorney from my uncle, which you must obtain under pretence of relieving him of the fatigues of business. Also, a month later, I must have a special power to transfer the income in the Funds. When that stands in my name, you and I have an equal interest in marrying each other. There it all is, my beautiful aunt, as plain as day. Between you and me there must be no ambiguity. I can marry my aunt at the end of a year’s widowhood; but I could not marry a disgraced girl.”

He left without waiting for an answer. Vedie, clearing the table, found her mistress pale and sweating despite the season. Flore felt as if she had fallen to the bottom of a precipice; the future seemed black and full of monstrous shapes. The damp chill of vaults, an instinctive fear of the man, oppressed her; yet a voice told her she deserved to have him for her master. She was helpless.

Flore Brazier had had a room of her own in Rouget’s house; now Madame Rouget belonged to her husband and was deprived of the freedom of a servant-mistress. In the horrible situation she discovered the hope of having a child, but soon saw that impossible under Philippe’s constant watch. Benjamin was Philippe’s innocent spy; Vedie trembled under him. Flore felt deserted and utterly helpless; she feared death. Without knowing how he might manage it, she felt certain that if he suspected pregnancy her doom would be sealed. The sound of his voice, the veiled glitter of his gambler’s eye, the slightest movement of the soldier—politeness cloaking brutality—made her shudder. The power of attorney he demanded he obtained as soon as he wanted it; Flore fell under his dominion as France had fallen under Napoleon.

Like a butterfly caught in wax, Rouget rapidly dissipated his remaining strength. In his decay the nephew remained as cold and impassible as diplomats in convulsed times.

Philippe, now skeptical of Napoleon II., wrote to the minister of war through intermediaries to offer his services to the King. He explained that Napoleon was gone; having been faithful, he was now free to serve the monarch. He asked for consideration and reinstatement at his rank, professing entire submission and assuring the minister of his fidelity. Accompanying the letter were requests for permission to go to Paris on urgent family business and endorsements from the mayor, the sub-prefect, and the commissary of police of Issoudun, praising Philippe’s conduct and citing newspaper articles about Rouget’s marriage.

Two weeks later, Philippe received permission and a letter informing him that by order of the King he was, as a preliminary favor, reinstated lieutenant-colonel in the royal army.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER XVII

Lieutenant-Colonel Bridau returned to Paris with his aunt and the helpless Rouget, whom he escorted three days after their arrival to the Treasury, where Jean-Jacques signed the transfer of the income, which henceforth became Philippe’s. The exhausted old man and the Rabouilleuse were now plunged by their nephew into the dissipations of actresses, journalists, artists, and equivocal women among whom Philippe had already wasted his youth; old Rouget found excitements that soon after killed him. Instigated by Giroudeau, Lolotte, one of the handsomest of the Opera ballet-girls, was the amiable assassin of the old man. Rouget died after a splendid supper at Florentine’s, and Lolotte threw the blame upon a slice of pâté de foie gras; as the Strasburg masterpiece could make no defence, it was considered settled that the old man died of indigestion.

Madame Rouget was in her element amid this decollete society; but Philippe gave her in charge of Mariette, and that monitress did not allow the widow—whose mourning was diversified with a few amusements—to commit any actual follies.

In October, 1823, Philippe returned to Issoudun, furnished with a power of attorney from his aunt, to liquidate his uncle’s estate; the business was soon over, and he returned to Paris in March, 1824, with sixteen hundred thousand francs—the net proceeds of old Rouget’s property, not counting the precious pictures, which had never left Monsieur Hochon’s hands. Philippe put the whole property into Mongenod and Sons, where young Baruch Borniche was employed, on Hochon’s assurances of solvency and probity. This house took his funds at six per cent per annum, on condition of three months’ notice for withdrawal.

One day Philippe went to see his mother and invited her to his marriage, witnessed by Giroudeau, Finot, Nathan, and Bixiou. By the marriage contract, the widow Rouget, whose portion amounted to a million of francs, secured to her future husband her whole fortune should she die without children. No invitations were sent; Philippe had designs. He lodged his wife in an appartement in the rue Saint-Georges, bought ready-furnished from Lolotte. Madame Bridau the younger thought it delightful; her husband rarely set foot in it. Without her knowledge, Philippe purchased in the rue de Clichy a magnificent hotel for two hundred and fifty thousand francs, paying one hundred and fifty thousand down and taking two years for the rest. He spent large sums altering the interior and furnishing it; in fact, he put his income for two years into this outlay. The restored pictures, then estimated at three hundred thousand francs, shone there.

The accession of Charles X. brought into greater court favor the family of the Duc de Chaulieu, whose eldest son, the Duc de Rhetore, was in the habit of seeing Philippe at Tullia’s. Under Charles X., the elder branch of the Bourbons, believing itself secure, followed advice to encourage adherence of former Imperial soldiers. Philippe, who had likely made invaluable revelations about the conspiracies of 1820 and 1822, was appointed lieutenant-colonel in the regiment of the Duc de Maufrigneuse. That nobleman felt bound to protect the man from whom he had taken Mariette. The corps-de-ballet went for something, therefore, in the appointment. Moreover, it was decided in private councils to give a tinge of liberalism to the Dauphin’s surroundings. Philippe, now a sort of equerry to the Duc de Maufrigneuse, was presented to the Dauphin and to the Dauphine, who liked brusque soldierly characters noted for past fidelity. Philippe understood the part the Dauphin had to play and turned this spurious liberalism to his profit by getting appointed aide-de-camp to a marshal who stood well at court.

In January, 1827, Philippe, now promoted in the Royal Guard as lieutenant-colonel in a regiment commanded by the Duc de Maufrigneuse, solicited ennoblement. Under the Restoration, nobility became a perquisite to “roturiers” serving in the Guard. Colonel Bridau had bought the estate of Brambourg and asked to entail it under the title of count. The favor was granted through his intimacies in high society, where he now appeared with the luxury of horses, carriages, and liveries; in short, the surroundings of a great lord. As soon as he saw himself gazetted as Comte de Brambourg, he began to frequent the house of a lieutenant-general of artillery, the Comte de Soulanges.

Insatiable and backed by mistresses of influential men, Philippe solicited the honor of being one of the Dauphin’s aides-de-camp. He had the audacity to tell the Dauphin that “an old soldier, wounded on many a battle-field and who knew real warfare, might, on occasion, be serviceable to Monseigneur.” Philippe, who could assume all tones of sycophancy, became in the highest social regions exactly what the position required; just as at Issoudun, he had copied Mignonnet’s respectability. He had a fine establishment and gave fêtes and dinners, admitting none of his old friends whose position might compromise him. He was pitiless to companions of former debauches and curtly refused Bixiou when that lively satirist asked him to say a word for Giroudeau, who wanted re-entry into the army after Florentine’s desertion.

“The man has neither manners nor morals,” said Philippe.

“Ha! did he say that of me?” cried Giroudeau. “Of me, who helped him get rid of his uncle!”

“We’ll pay him off yet,” said Bixiou.

Philippe intended to marry Mademoiselle Amelie de Soulanges and become a general commanding a Guard regiment. He asked so many favors that, to keep him quiet, they made him a Commander of the Legion of Honor and of Saint Louis. One rainy evening, as Agathe and Joseph returned home along the muddy streets, they met Philippe in full uniform, bedizened with orders, leaning back in a coupe lined with yellow silk, whose armorial bearings were surmounted with a count’s coronet. He was on his way to a fête at the Élysée-Bourbon; the wheels splashed his mother and brother as he waved a patronizing greeting.

“He’s going it, that fellow!” said Joseph. “Nevertheless, he might send us something better than mud in our faces.”

“He has such a fine position, in such high society, that we ought not to blame him for forgetting us,” said Madame Bridau. “When a man rises to so great a height, he has obligations to repay, sacrifices to make; it is natural he should not come to see us, though he may think of us all the same.”

“My dear fellow,” said the Duc de Maufrigneuse one evening to the new Comte de Brambourg, “I am sure your addresses will be favorably received; but to marry Amelie de Soulanges you must be free. What have you done with your wife?”

“My wife?” said Philippe, with a gesture, look, and accent worthy of Frederick Lemaitre. “Alas! I have the melancholy certainty of losing her. She has not a week to live. My dear duke, you don’t know what it is to marry beneath you. A woman who was a cook, who has the tastes of a cook! Who dishonors me—ah! I am much to be pitied. I explained my position to Madame la Dauphine. At the time of the marriage, it was a question of saving the family a million which my uncle left to that person. Happily, my wife took to drinking; at her death, I come into possession of that million, which is now in Mongenod and Sons. I have thirty thousand francs a year in the five per cents, and my entailed landed property brings forty thousand more. If Monsieur de Soulanges gets a marshal’s baton, I am on the high-road, with my title, to becoming general and peer of France. That will be the proper end of an aide-de-camp of the Dauphin.”

After the Salon of 1823, one of the leading painters obtained the management of a lottery-office near the Markets for Joseph Bridau’s mother. Agathe was able soon after to exchange it for an office in the rue de Seine, in a house where Joseph had his atelier. The widow now hired an agent herself and was no longer an expense to her son. And yet, as late as 1828, though she directed an office owed to Joseph’s fame, Madame Bridau still doubted that fame, which was hotly contested as all true glory is. The great painter, struggling with genius, had enormous wants; he did not earn enough to pay for luxuries that his relations and position in the young School of Art demanded. Though sustained by his Cenacle friends and by Mademoiselle des Touches, he did not please the bourgeoisie. The bourgeoisie, from whom money comes, never unties its purse for genius that is in question; unfortunately, Joseph had the classics and the Institute and their critics against him. Though backed by Gros and Géricault (Gerard), by whose influence he was decorated after the Salon of 1827, he obtained few orders. Moreover, Joseph gave way too much to imaginative fancies, producing inequality in his work which enemies used to deny his talent.

“High art is at a low ebb,” said his friend Pierre Grassou, who made daubs for bourgeois tastes.

“You ought to have a whole cathedral to decorate; that’s what you want,” declared Schinner; “then you would silence criticism with a master-stroke.”

Such speeches alarmed Agathe and confirmed her long-held judgment about Philippe and Joseph. Facts sustained that judgment in a provincial woman’s mind. Philippe, her favorite, was now a great man; his early errors were only youth’s ebullitions. Joseph, whose productions she could not appreciate—having seen them in progress too often—seemed no more advanced in 1828 than in 1816. Poor Joseph owed money and was bowed by debt; she felt he had chosen a worthless career that gave no return. She could not conceive why they had given him the Legion cross. Philippe, rich enough to cease gambling, a guest at Madame’s fêtes, the brilliant colonel appearing at reviews and processions in splendid uniforms with two crosses, realized all her maternal dreams. One day of public ceremony effaced from Agathe’s mind the sight of Philippe’s earlier misery; on that day he passed her at the same spot, in attendance on the Dauphin, with plumes in his shako and a pelisse gorgeous with gold and fur. Agathe, who to her artist son was now a devoted sister-figure, felt herself the mother of none but the dashing aide-de-camp. Proud of Philippe, she felt he made her life easy and happy—forgetting that the lottery-office enabling her to live came through Joseph.

One day Agathe noticed Joseph more worried than usual by his color-man’s bill and resolved, though cursing his profession in her heart, to free him from debts. She kept the house with the office’s proceeds and never asked Joseph for a farthing; she had no money of her own but relied on Philippe’s good heart and purse. For three years she had expected his visits; she imagined that if she appealed he would bring an enormous sum, and pictured the happiness of giving it to Joseph, whose judgment of his brother she found unfair.

Without telling Joseph, she wrote the following letter to Philippe:

  To Monsieur le comte de Brambourg:

  My dear Philippe,—You have not given the least little word of remembrance to your mother for five years. That is not right. You should remember the past, if only for the sake of your excellent brother. Joseph is now in need of money, and you are floating in wealth; he works, while you are flying from fête to fête. You now possess, all to yourself, the property of my brother. Little Borniche tells me you cannot have less than two hundred thousand francs a year. Well, then, come and see Joseph. During your visit, slip into the skull a few thousand-franc notes. Philippe, you owe them to us; nevertheless, your brother will feel grateful to you, not to speak of the happiness you will give

  Your mother,

  Agathe Bridau, nee Rouget

Two days later the concierge brought to the atelier the terrible reply:

  My dear Mother,—A man does not marry a Mademoiselle Amelie de Soulanges without the purse of Fortunatus, if under the name of Comte de Brambourg he hides that of

  Your son,

  Philippe Bridau

Agathe fell half-fainting on the sofa; the letter dropped. The slight noise and her smothered exclamation startled Joseph, who had been rubbing in a sketch. He leaned his head round the canvas, saw his mother stretched out, and rushed to lift what seemed lifeless. He carried Agathe to her bed and sent for Horace Bianchon. When he could question her she told him of her letter and its reply. Joseph went to the atelier, picked up Philippe’s concise brutality, which had broken his mother’s heart and shattered the edifice of trust. When Joseph returned to her bedside he was silent; he did not speak of his brother during the three weeks of the poor woman’s death agony. Bianchon, who watched with devotion, told Joseph the truth on the first day of her seizure.

“At her age,” he said, “and under the circumstances, all we can hope is to make her death as little painful as possible.”

She herself felt so surely called that she asked for Abbe Loraux, her confessor for over twenty-two years. Alone with him, having poured out her griefs, she said—as she had said to Madame Hochon and repeated to herself—“What have I done to displease God? Have I not loved Him with all my soul? Have I wandered from the path of grace? What is my sin? Can I be guilty of wrong when I know not what it is? Have I time to repair it?”

“No,” said the old man gently. “Alas! your life seems pure and your soul spotless; but the eye of God is keener than that of his ministers. I see the truth too late; for you have misled even me.”

Hearing such words from lips always peaceful, Agathe rose suddenly and opened wide her eyes in terror.

“Tell me! tell me!” she cried.

“Be comforted,” said the priest. “Your punishment is a proof that you will receive pardon. God chastens his elect. Woe to those whose misdeeds meet with fortunate success; they will be kneaded again in humanity until sorely punished and brought to maturity. Your life, my daughter, has been one long error. You gave your heart to an unnatural son, in whom you made your glory, and you have misunderstood the child who is your true glory. You have been so unjust that you never saw the striking contrast between the brothers. You owe the comfort of your life to Joseph, while your other son has pillaged you repeatedly. The poor son, who loves you without equal return, gives you all the comfort your life has had; the rich son, who never thinks of you, despises you and desires your death—”

“Oh no,” she cried.

“Yes,” resumed the priest. “Your humble position stands in the way of his proud hopes. Mother, these are your sins! You have sinned by ignorance. Love Joseph now; give him your heart and pray for him, as I shall for you.”

The mother’s eyes opened to her life’s course. Illuminated, she wept. The old priest, moved by her repentance, left the room hastily.

Joseph returned about two hours after the confessor had left. He had been to borrow the money needed to pay pressing debts and sat quietly, thinking his mother asleep. He sprang up with a chill as he heard her say, brokenly, “Will he forgive me?”

“What is it, mother?” he exclaimed, shocked at her expression.

“Ah, Joseph! can you pardon me, my child?” she cried.

“For what?” he said.

“I have never loved you as you deserved to be loved.”

“Oh, what an accusation!” he cried. “Not loved me? For seven years have we not lived alone together? Have you not taken care of me? Are you not my gentle and indulgent companion? I was saying to Grassou only yesterday: ‘What comforts me is that I have such a good mother. She sees to everything; she takes care of my material wants without troubling me.’”

“No, Joseph, no; you have loved me, but I have not returned love for love. Ah! would I could live a little longer—Give me your hand.”

Agathe took his hand, kissed it, held it to her heart, and looked long at him, letting him see tenderness she had hitherto bestowed only on Philippe. The painter, apt at judging expression, saw how his mother’s heart opened and took her to his breast, crying, “My mother! oh, my mother!”

“Ah! I feel that I am forgiven!” she said. “God will confirm the child’s pardon of its mother.”

“You must be calm: don’t torment yourself; hear me. I feel loved enough in this one moment for all the past,” he said, laying her back on pillows.

During the two weeks’ struggle between life and death, each effusion of feeling seemed an expression of a lifetime. The mother thought only of her son; sustained by love, she was unaware of her sufferings. D’Arthez, Michel Chrestien, Fulgence Ridal, Pierre Grassou, and Bianchon often kept Joseph company, the friends serving as a band of brothers in thought and action. Joseph, inspired by feeling and genius, divined in his mother’s occasional expressions a desire hidden in her heart and said to d’Arthez, “She has loved that brigand Philippe too well not to want to see him before she dies.”

Joseph begged Bixiou, who frequented Bohemian regions where Philippe was still occasionally found, to persuade that shameless son to play, out of pity, a little comedy that might fold the mother in an illusory peace. Bixiou, an observing misanthropic scoffer, undertook the mission. When he told the Comte de Brambourg—who received him in a bedroom hung with yellow damask—Philippe laughed.

“What the devil do you want me to do there?” he cried. “The only service the poor woman can render me is to die as soon as she can; she would be a sorry figure at my marriage with Mademoiselle de Soulanges. The less my family is seen, the better my position. You can easily understand that I should like to bury the name of Bridau under all the monuments in Père-Lachaise. My brother irritates me by bringing the name into publicity. Look at it as if it were your own: if you were a deputy, with a tongue like yours, you would be as much feared as Chauvelin; you would be made Comte Bixiou, and director of the Beaux-Arts. Once there, how should you like it if your grandmother Descoings were to turn up? Would you want that worthy woman hanging on you? Damn it, you’d wish her six feet under ground. Come, breakfast with me, and let us talk of something else. I am a parvenu, my dear fellow, and I know it. I don’t choose that my swaddling-clothes shall be seen. My son will be more fortunate than I. The scamp will wish me dead; I expect it—or he won’t be my son.”

He rang for breakfast. “The fashionable world wouldn’t see you in your mother’s bedroom,” said Bixiou. “What would it cost you to seem to love that poor woman for a few hours?”

“Whew!” cried Philippe, winking. “So you come from them, do you? I’m an old camel, who knows all about genuflections. My mother makes the excuse of her last illness to get something out of me for Joseph. No, thank you!”

When Bixiou related this to Joseph, the poor painter was chilled to the soul.

“Does Philippe know I am ill?” asked Agathe the day after Bixiou’s errand.

Joseph left, suffocating with emotion. The Abbe Loraux, sitting by the bed, took her hand and said, “Alas! my child, you have never had but one son.” The words, which Agathe understood, conveyed a shock that began the end. She died twenty hours later.

In the delirium preceding death, she murmured, “Whom does Philippe take after?”

Joseph followed his mother to the grave alone. Philippe had gone on business to Orléans; in reality he was driven from Paris by the letter Joseph wrote a moment after Agathe’s last sigh:

  Monster! my poor mother has died of the shock your letter caused her. Wear mourning, but pretend illness; I will not suffer her assassin to stand at my side before her coffin.

  Joseph B.

Joseph, who no longer had the heart to paint though grief needed the distraction of labor, was surrounded by friends who vowed never to leave him alone. Thus, two weeks after Agathe’s funeral, Bixiou sat in the atelier with a group when the servant brought a letter from an old woman below.

  Monsieur,—To you, whom I scarcely dare to call my brother, I am forced to address myself, if only on account of the name I bear.--

Joseph turned the page and read the signature: “Comtesse Flore de Brambourg.” He shuddered, foreseeing new atrocity from his brother.

“That brigand,” he cried, “is the devil’s own. And he calls himself a man of honor! And he wears crosses on his breast! And he struts at court instead of being bastinadoed! And the scoundrel is called Monsieur le Comte!”

“There are many like him,” said Bixiou.

“After all,” said Joseph, “the Rabouilleuse deserves her fate. She’s not worth pitying; she’d have had my neck wrung without saying, ‘He’s innocent.’”

Joseph flung the letter; Bixiou caught and read it aloud:

  Is it decent that the Comtesse Bridau de Brambourg should die in a hospital, no matter what her faults? If so, will you, who are the friend of Doctor Bianchon, ask him for a permit to let me enter a hospital?

  The person who carries this letter has been eleven consecutive days to the hotel de Brambourg, rue de Clichy, without getting any help from my husband. Poverty prevents me from employing a lawyer to demand what is due me, that I may die with decency. Nothing can save me, I know that. If you are unwilling to see your unhappy sister-in-law, send me, at least, the money to end my days. Your brother desires my death; he has always desired it. He warned me that he knew three ways of killing a woman, but I had not the sense to foresee the one he has employed.

  In case you will relieve me and judge for yourself my misery, I live in the rue du Houssay, corner of the rue Chantereine, on the fifth floor. If I cannot pay my rent to-morrow I shall be put out--and then, where can I go?

  Your sister-in-law,

  Comtesse Flore de Brambourg.

“What a pit of infamy!” cried Joseph; “there is something under it all.”

“Let us send for the woman who brought the letter; we may get the preface,” said Bixiou.

The woman appeared, a mass of old gowns fringed with mud, mounted on thick legs with ragged stockings and leaking shoes. Above the mound rose a head like Charlet’s scavenger-women, caparisoned with a filthy bandanna.

“What is your name?” said Joseph as Bixiou sketched her, leaning on an umbrella dated year II.

“Madame Gruget, at your service. I’ve seen better days,” she said. “If my poor girl hadn’t loved some one too much, you wouldn’t see me what I am. She drowned herself in the river, my poor Ida,—saving your presence! I’ve had the folly to nurse up a quaterne, and that’s why, at seventy-seven, I’m obliged to take care of sick folks for ten sous a day, and go—”

“—without clothes?” said Bixiou. “My grandmother nursed up a trey, but she dressed properly.”

“Out of my ten sous I pay for lodging—”

“What’s wrong with the lady you nurse?”

“In the first place, she hasn’t any money; and she has a disease that scares doctors. She owes me for sixty days’ nursing; that’s why I keep on. The husband, who is a count—she is really a countess—will no doubt pay me when she is dead; so I’ve lent her all I had. I haven’t anything left; all I did have has gone to the pawn-brokers. She owes me forty-seven francs and twelve sous, besides thirty francs for nursing. She wants to kill herself with charcoal. I tell her it ain’t right; I’ve had to get the concierge to look after her while I’m gone, or she’ll jump out the window.”

“But what’s the matter with her?” said Joseph.

“Ah! monsieur, the doctor from the Sisters’ hospital came; but as to the disease,” said Madame Gruget, modestly, “he told me she must go to the hospital. The case is hopeless.”

“Let us go and see her,” said Bixiou.

“Here,” said Joseph, “take these ten francs.”

Plunging his hand into his skull and taking out all his remaining money, Joseph called a coach and went for Bianchon, who was fortunately home. Bixiou ran to the rue de Bussy for Desroches. The four friends reached Flore’s retreat in the rue du Houssay an hour later.

“That Mephistopheles on horseback, named Philippe Bridau,” said Bixiou as they climbed the steep stair, “has sailed his boat cleverly to get rid of his wife. You know Lousteau? well, Philippe paid him a thousand francs a month to keep Madame Bridau in the society of Florine, Mariette, Tullia, and the Val-Noble. When Philippe saw his crab-girl so used to pleasure and dress that she couldn’t do without them, he stopped paying and left her to get it as she could—it is easy to know how. By the end of eighteen months the brute forced his wife lower and lower; then, by the help of a young officer, he gave her a taste for drinking. As he rose, his wife sank; the countess is now in the mud. I don’t know what means Philippe has lately taken to get rid of her. I am anxious to study this drama, for I am determined to avenge Joseph. Alas, friends,” he added, leaving them in doubt whether jesting, “give a man over to a vice and you’ll get rid of him. Didn’t Hugo say: ‘She loved a ball, and died of it’? So it is. Vice! vice! my dear friends, do you want to know what vice is? It is the Bonneau of death.”

“Then you’ll die of a joke,” said Desroches, laughing.

Above the fourth floor, they climbed a ladder-like attic stair. Though Joseph remembered Flore’s beauty, he was unprepared for the hideous spectacle. In a room with bare walls under the attic roof, on a cot with a scanty mattress, a woman lay green as a body drowned, thin as a consumptive. Her head, nearly hairless, was bound with a checked handkerchief; circles round the hollow eyes were red; eyelids like the pellicle of an egg. Nothing remained of the captivating body but an ignoble bony structure. As Flore saw Joseph, two great tears rolled down her cheeks.

“She can still weep!” whispered Bixiou. “A strange sight—tears from dominos! It’s like the miracle of Moses.”

“How burnt up!” cried Joseph.

“In the fires of repentance,” said Flore. “I cannot get a priest; I have nothing, not even a crucifix, to help me see God. Ah, monsieur!” she cried, raising arms like carved wood, “I am a guilty woman; but God never punished any one as he has punished me! Philippe killed Max, who advised dreadful things, and now he has killed me. God uses him as a scourge!”

“Leave me alone with her,” said Bianchon, “and let me see if the disease is curable.”

“If you cure her, Philippe Bridau will die of rage,” said Desroches. “I will draw up a statement of her condition. He has not brought her before the courts as an adulteress, and thus her conjugal rights stand: he shall have the shame of a suit. But first we must remove the Comtesse to Doctor Dubois’s private hospital in the rue du Faubourg-Saint-Denis. She will be cared for there. Then I will summon the count for restoration of the conjugal home.”

“Bravo, Desroches!” cried Bixiou. “What a pleasure to do so much good that will make some people feel so badly!”

Ten minutes later Bianchon joined them. “I am going straight to Despleins,” he said. “He can save her by an operation. Ah! he will take care of the case, for her abuse of liquor has developed a disease thought lost.”

“Wag of a mangler! Isn’t there but one disease in life?” cried Bixiou.

Bianchon was already gone. Two hours later Flore was removed to Dubois’s hospital. Three weeks later the “Hospital Gazette” published an account of one of modern surgery’s boldest operations on a case designated by the initials “F. B.” The patient died—more from exhaustion produced by misery and starvation than from treatment.

No sooner did she die than the Comte de Brambourg went, in deep mourning, to call on the Comte de Soulanges and inform him of his sad loss. Soon after, fashion whispered that the Comte de Soulanges would marry his daughter to a parvenu of merit about to be appointed brigadier-general and given command of a Royal Guard regiment. De Marsay told Eugene de Rastignac this news at the Rocher de Cancale, where Bixiou happened to be.

“It shall not take place!” said the witty artist to himself.

Among the many old friends Philippe refused to recognize were some unable to revenge themselves; but he had wounded Bixiou, who, thanks to brilliant qualities, was everywhere received and never forgave an insult. One day at the Rocher de Cancale, before many well-bred people, Philippe had replied to Bixiou—who spoke of visiting him at the hotel de Brambourg—“You can come and see me when you are made a minister.”

“Am I to turn Protestant before I can visit you?” said Bixiou, feigning misunderstanding; but he thought, “You may be Goliath, but I have my sling.”

The next day he went to an actor friend, metamorphosed himself by dress into a secularized priest with green spectacles, drove to the hotel de Soulanges, and, received by the count on a message that he had serious business, related in a feigned voice the whole story of the dead countess and the secret particulars confided to him by Bianchon; the histories of Agathe’s death, of old Rouget’s death (which the Comte had boasted of), of Madame Descoings’s death, of the theft from the newspaper, and of Philippe’s early private morals.

“Monsieur le comte, don’t give him your daughter until you have made every inquiry; interrogate his former comrades,—Bixiou, Giroudeau, and others.”

Three months later the Comte de Brambourg gave a supper to du Tillet, Nucingen, Eugene de Rastignac, Maxime de Trailles, and Henri de Marsay. The host accepted their half-consolatory condolences about his rupture with Soulanges with nonchalance.

“You can do better,” said Maxime de Trailles.

“How much must a man have to marry a demoiselle de Grandlieu?” asked Philippe of de Marsay.

“You? They wouldn’t give you the ugliest of the six for less than ten millions,” answered de Marsay insolently.

“Bah!” said Rastignac. “With an income of two hundred thousand francs you can have Mademoiselle de Langeais; she is thirty, ugly, and penniless; that ought to suit you.”

“I shall have ten millions two years from now,” said Philippe Bridau.

“It is now the 16th of January, 1829,” cried du Tillet, laughing. “I have been hard at work for ten years and have not made as much as that yet.”

“We’ll take counsel of each other,” said Bridau; “you shall see how well I understand finance.”

“How much do you really own?” asked Nucingen.

“Three millions, excluding my house and estate, which I shall not sell; in fact, I cannot, for the property is now entailed and goes with the title.”

Nucingen and du Tillet exchanged a sly glance; du Tillet said to Philippe, “My dear count, I shall be delighted to do business with you.”

De Marsay intercepted the look, which meant, “We will have those millions.” The two bank magnates were central in political affairs and could, at a given time, manipulate matters at the Bourse to play a sure game against Philippe when probabilities seemed for him but were secretly against him.

The occasion came. In July, 1830, du Tillet and Nucingen had helped the Comte de Brambourg make fifteen hundred thousand francs; he trusted them. Philippe, owing his rise to the Restoration, was misled by profound contempt for “civilians”; he believed in the triumph of the Ordonnances and was bent on playing for a rise; du Tillet and Nucingen, sure of a revolution, played against him for a fall. The crafty pair confirmed his judgment and seemed to share his convictions; they encouraged his hopes of doubling his millions and apparently took steps to help. Philippe fought like a man with four millions depending on the struggle. His devotion was so noticeable that he received orders to go to Saint-Cloud with the Duc de Maufrigneuse and attend a council. This mark probably saved his life; for when the order came on July 25th he was intending to charge and sweep the boulevards, where he would undoubtedly have been shot by his friend Giroudeau, who commanded a division of attackers.

A month later nothing was left of Colonel Bridau’s immense fortune but his house and furniture, his estates, and the pictures from Issoudun. He committed the folly of believing in the Restoration of the elder branch, to which he remained faithful until 1834. Jealous of Giroudeau’s colonelcy, he re-entered the service. Unluckily, in 1835 he obtained command of a regiment in Algeria, where he remained three dangerous years, always hoping for a general’s epaulets. But some malignant influence—General Giroudeau—continually balked him. Grown hard and brutal, Philippe exceeded ordinary severity and was hated, despite bravery à la Murat.

At the beginning of 1839, while making a dash upon Arabs during a retreat before superior forces, he flung himself against the enemy with only a single company and fell in with the main body. The battle was bloody and terrible, man to man, and few horsemen escaped. Seeing their colonel surrounded, these men, at some distance, were unwilling to perish attempting to rescue him. They heard his cry: “Your colonel! to me! a colonel of the Empire!” but they rejoined the regiment. Philippe met a horrible death: the Arabs hacked him to pieces with scimitars and cut off his head.

Joseph, who was married about this time through Comte de Serizy to the daughter of a millionaire farmer, inherited his brother’s Paris house and the estate of Brambourg by the entail, which Philippe, had he foreseen this, would have broken. The chief pleasure Joseph derived from his inheritance was the fine collection of paintings from Issoudun. He now possesses an income of sixty thousand francs, and his father-in-law continues to pile up five-franc pieces. Though Joseph Bridau paints magnificent pictures and renders important services to artists, he is not yet a member of the Institute. As result of a clause in the deed of entail, he is now Comte de Brambourg, a fact which often makes him roar with laughter among his atelier friends.
